Two Separate Oaths with a Gap Do Not Constitute Ila
Ruling on two oaths of abstinence with a gap

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on two separate oaths of abstinence with a gap and apply the condition of continuity to determine whether Ila is formed.

Core Idea
A gap between oaths breaks continuity; Ila requires four continuous months.

In the Hanafi school, Ila (a vow of abstinence) is only established when a husband swears to avoid intercourse with his wife for a continuous period of four months or more. But what if he makes two separate oaths with a time interval between them? This lesson examines a key ruling from the chapter of Ila that clarifies when such oaths do—or do not—create Ila. You will learn to distinguish between combined and separate oaths and understand why a gap prevents the four-month continuity required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
The passage rules on whether two oaths of abstinence separated by a time gap (e.g., a day) constitute Ila. The answer: they do not. Identify the act (two separate oaths) and the consequence (no Ila).
The ruling is based on the condition of continuity.
2
Understand the condition of continuity for Ila
Ila requires a single, uninterrupted period of abstention of at least four months. If the oaths are separate with a gap, the abstention is not continuous.
The gap can be any length—even an hour, as the commentator notes.
3
Distinguish between combined and separate oaths
When two durations are joined by 'and' (e.g., 'two months and two months'), it forms one oath with cumulative duration. When repeated with 'or' or separate 'by Allah', they are two oaths with overlapping periods.
This distinction affects expiation (kaffarah) as well.
4
Apply the principle to the given examples
Work through the scenario: first oath for two months, then after a day a second oath for two more months. The total abstention is not continuous, so no Ila. Similarly, separate oaths of speaking to Zayd.
The same principle applies to any act, not just Ila.
5
Recognize the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies whenever there is any interval between the oaths. The 'day' in the example is just an illustration; even an hour suffices.
The phrase 'after the first two months' is incidental; the ruling does not depend on it.

[٣] قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ مدّة الامتناع في الحلف الأوّل كان شهرين ، وفي الحلف الثاني شهران بعدهما، وبين الحلفين مدّة لم يلزمه شيء بالقربان فيها، وهو الزمان الفاصل بينهما ، كاليوم مثلاً ، فلم توجد مدة الإيلاء، وهي أربعة أشهر، بخلافِ
المسألة السابقة فإنّه لا فاصل هناك.
Contrary to after a day: 'By Allah I will not approach you for two months after the first two months' contrary to after a day: meaning if he said 'By Allah I will not approach you for two months' and stayed a day, then said 'By Allah I will not approach you for two months after the first two months', he is not a moli because on the first day his oath was for two months, on the second day his oath was for four months except one day. On the third day he does not break the oath because its term has expired. Similarly, if he said 'By Allah I will not speak to Zayd for two days' and 'By Allah I will not speak to Zayd for two days'. And if he said 'By Allah I will not speak to him for two days and two days' it is one oath, its duration is four days, so if he speaks to him during it, one expiation is due. And if he said 'By Allah I will not speak to him for a day or two days' or said 'By Allah I will not speak to him for a day' and 'By Allah I will not speak to him for two days', they are two oaths: the duration of the first is one day, the duration of the second is two days. So if he speaks to him on the first day, two expiations are due; on the second day, one expiation; and if he speaks to him on the third day, he does not break the oath because its term has expired. And based on this, if he said 'By Allah I will not approach you for two months nor two months' or said 'By Allah I will not approach you for two months' and 'By Allah I will not approach you for two months', he is not a moli because they are two oaths, so their durations overlap. Thus mentioned by al-Zayla'i in al-Tabyin. [1] He said: 'after a day' is by way of example, otherwise an hour is also the same. In summary, if there is a separation between them by an interval, he is not a moli. [2] He said: 'after the first two months' is also a contingent condition; the ruling does not differ if he did not say this word. [3] He said: 'because' etc. Its summary: the period of abstention in the first oath was two months, and in the second oath two months after them, and between the two oaths there was a duration in which he was not bound by anything regarding approaching her, which is the intervening time, like a day for example. Therefore the Ila period, which is four months, was not present, unlike the previous issue where there is no interval.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter of Ila in the book of Talaq. The passage addresses when two oaths of abstinence are made with a time gap between them. The author explains that if a man swears an oath to abstain from his wife for two months, then after a day he swears another oath for two months after the first two, he does not become a moli (one who makes Ila). The reason is that the first oath ends after two months, and the second oath starts after a gap, so the total period of abstention is not a continuous four months—the essential condition for Ila. The same principle applies to other oaths, like speaking to someone. The passage also distinguishes between a single oath combined with 'and' (making one oath with cumulative duration) and separate oaths with 'or' or repeated 'by Allah' (making two separate oaths). In the case of two separate oaths regarding Ila, the durations overlap, so again no Ila. The commentator clarifies that the mention of 'day' is just an example—any interval suffices—and the phrase 'after the first two months' is incidental; the ruling would be the same without it. The key point is that a continuous four-month abstention is required for Ila; any break in the continuity prevents it.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-ilā'): A vow of abstinence from sexual intercourse with one's wife for a minimum of four months, entitling the wife to seek judicial recourse after four months.
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation for breaking an oath, which in Hanafi law involves feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave, or fasting three days if unable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears an oath to abstain for two months, then after one day swears another oath for two months after the first two, does he become a moli?
A) Yes, because the total period is four months.
B) No, because the periods are separated by a gap.
C) Yes, but only if he intended Ila from the beginning.
D) No, because the second oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Ila requires a single, continuous four-month period of abstention; any gap between separate oaths prevents Ila.

When Vows Do Not Create Ila: Indefinite Exception and Location-Based Vows
Rulings on vows that do not create Ila due to indefinite exception or location condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between vows that create Ila and those that do not, specifically when the exception is indefinite or the vow is conditioned on a location, and state the Hanafi position in the dispute with Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
An indefinite exception in a vow of abstinence prevents continuous four-month abstention, thus no Ila.

Ila is a form of divorce triggered by a husband swearing to abstain from intercourse with his wife for four months or more. However, not every vow of abstinence qualifies. This passage examines two scenarios where the vow does not meet the strict conditions for Ila. The first involves a year-long vow with an indefinite exception of one day; the second involves a vow not to enter a city where the wife resides. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying the rules of Ila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Read the Arabic text and note the two distinct scenarios: (a) a vow to abstain for a year except one undefined day, and (b) a vow not to enter Kufa while the wife is there.
Both are claims where Ila might be suspected but is ruled out.
2
Analyse the first case: the indefinite exception
The husband said 'except a day' without specifying which day. In Hanafi usul, an indefinite exception (الاستثناء المنكر) applies to any possible instance. Thus, he can choose to fulfill the exception on the first day, breaking the continuous four-month abstention.
Contrast with an exception that is definite or specified, which might create continuity.
3
Examine Zufar's opposing view
Imam Zufar interprets the exception as referring to the last day of the year, thereby making the abstention continuous for the year except the last day. This would meet the four-month condition.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the Hanafi tarjih
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) reject Zufar's view because there is no evidence to fix the exception to the last day. The indefinite nature of the exception prevents such a specification.
The text also mentions an exception 'except a decrease of a day' which idiomatically refers to the end and would create Ila.
5
Analyse the second case: location-based vow
The husband swore not to enter Kufa while the wife is there. But he can have intercourse with her outside Kufa, either by her leaving or by meeting elsewhere. The vow does not prohibit intercourse itself.
Thus, no abstention from intercourse, so no Ila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً، وقولُهُ بالبصرة : والله لا أدخل الكوفة، وامرأته بها.
[١] قوله : وقوله ؛ هذا مع ما يقارنه معطوف على قوله : «أي» بخلاف قوله : والله لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً»، وبخلاف قوله : «بالبصرة» مثلاً وامرأته بالكوفة» : «والله لا أدخل الكوفة فلا إيلاء في هاتين الصورتين أيضاً.
(۲)
أما في المسألة الأولى فلأنه استثنى يوماً منكراً فيصدق على كل يوم من أيام السنة حقيقة، فيمكنه قربانها قبل مضي أربعة أشهر من غير شيء يلزمه، والمولى من لا يمكنه القربان أربعة أشهر لا بشيء يلزمه، وفيه خلاف زفر ، فإنه يصرف الاستثناء إلى الأخير، فتتم مدة المنع. وجوابه : إنه لا دليل عليه ؛ لكون المستثنى منكراً ، نعم لو قال : إلا نقصان يوم يكون مولياً ؛ لأنّ النقصان عرفاً لا يكون إلا من الآخر. كذا في «البحر ) وأما في المسألة الثانية ؛ فلأنه يمكنه وطؤها في المدة بخروجها من الكوفة، فلم يكن قوله امتناعاً عن الوطء.
And his saying: 'By Allah I will not approach you for a year except a day', and his saying in Basra: 'By Allah I will not enter Kufa' while his wife is there. [1] He said: 'And his saying' - this together with what is conjoined is connected to his saying 'i.e.' contrary to his saying: 'By Allah I will not approach you for a year except a day', and contrary to his saying 'In Basra' for example while his wife is in Kufa: 'By Allah I will not enter Kufa' - so there is no Ila in these two cases either. As for the first issue, because he excepted an indefinite day, it applies truly to every day of the year, so he can approach her before four months pass without anything being incumbent on him. And a moli is one who cannot approach her for four months without something being incumbent on him. There is a difference of opinion with Zufar, for he interprets the exception as applying to the last day, so the period of prohibition is complete. The response: There is no evidence for this because the excepted day is indefinite. Indeed, if he said 'except a decrease of a day' he would be a moli because a decrease conventionally only applies to the end. Thus in al-Bahr. As for the second issue, because he can have intercourse with her in that period by her leaving Kufa, so his statement was not an abstention from intercourse.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents two additional cases where a husband's vow does not constitute Ila. The first case is when a man swears to abstain from his wife for a year except one day. The exception of an indefinite day means that he can fulfill his vow by approaching her on any single day within the year. Since he can approach her before four months pass (e.g., on the first day), he is not prevented from intercourse for a continuous four months, which is the defining condition of Ila. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Zufar holds that the exception is applied to the last day of the year, so the abstention becomes continuous for the year minus one day, which would satisfy four months. The Hanafi response is that Zufar's view lacks evidence because the exception is indefinite (munkar); it does not necessarily refer to the end. However, if the phrase were 'except a decrease of a day', then it would be a moli because 'decrease' idiomatically refers to the end. The second case is when a man swears in Basra that he will not enter Kufa while his wife is in Kufa. Here, Ila does not apply because it is possible for him to have intercourse with her in the period by her leaving Kufa (i.e., he can go to her elsewhere, or she can come to him). Since he has the ability to approach her without entering Kufa, the vow does not constitute an abstention from intercourse altogether.

Key Terms
الاستثناء المنكر (al-istithnā' al-munkar): An indefinite exception, where the excepted item is not specified, thus applying to any possible instance.
النقصان (al-nuqsān): A decrease; in the context of vows, 'except a decrease of a day' idiomatically means the last day is excepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a man says to his wife 'By Allah I will not approach you for a year except one day'?
A) He becomes a moli for one year.
B) He does not become a moli because the exception applies to any day, so he can approach her before four months.
C) He becomes a moli for one day.
D) He becomes a moli for four months.

Key Takeaway
A vow of abstinence with an indefinite exception does not create Ila because the exception breaks the continuity required for Ila.

Invalidity of Ila from a Ba'in Divorcee and an Ajnabiyah
States the invalidity of Ila in specific relationships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions necessary for a valid Ila and identify when Ila is invalid due to absence of marriage.

Core Idea
Ila requires a current wife; a final divorce severs the marriage so Ila does not apply.

The passage explains that the vow of abstinence (Ila) is only valid when directed at a current wife. It clarifies two cases where Ila is invalid: a woman divorced with a final divorce (ba'in) and an unmarried woman (ajnabiyah). This ruling highlights the foundational requirement of an ongoing marriage for Ila to take effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of women for whom Ila is invalid
The passage explicitly states that there is no Ila from a mubainah (final divorcee) and an ajnabiyah (unrelated woman) whom he marries later. This is the core ruling.
These are distinct from a revocably divorced woman who is still a wife during iddah.
2
Understand the reasoning for each category
For a mubainah, the marriage is severed, so she is not a wife. For an ajnabiyah, there is no marriage at the time of the vow. The Ila vow must address a current wife.
The commentary contrasts with revocable divorce where the marriage continues.
3
Examine the exception for ajnabiyah
Ila can be effective regarding an ajnabiyah if the vow is attached to the marriage itself, similar to a suspended divorce (e.g., 'If I marry Zainab, I will abstain from her for four months').
This exception is noted in the passage as 'except if he attaches the Ila to marriage'.
4
Consider the effect of a ba'in divorce after Ila
If a man makes an Ila and then divorces his wife with a final divorce, the Ila may still take effect if its period expires while she is in iddah. If not, the Ila lapses.
This shows that the timing relative to iddah matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا إيلاء من مبائنة وأجنبية نكحها بعد ذلك
(٢) قوله : ولا إيلاء... الخ ؛ أما عدم الإيلاء من مبانته ؛ أي التي طلقها طلاقاً بائناً ؛ فلأن الإيلاء إنما يكون مع الزوجة، ولا زوجية في المبانة، بخلاف مطلقة الرجعي، فإنّ الزوجية هناك قائمة، فيصح الإيلاء.
[٣] قوله : بعد ذلك ؛ أي بعد الإيلاء ، إلا إذا أضاف الإيلاء إلى الملكِ كما مرّ في الطلاق المعلق، ولو الى أولاً ثمّ أبانها، فإن مضت مدته وهي في العدة بانت بأخرى ، وإلا لا. كذا في «الفتاوى الخانية»، وغيرها.
And there is no Ila from a mubainah (woman divorced with a final divorce) and an ajnabiyah (foreign woman) whom he marries after that. (2) He said: 'And no Ila' etc. As for the lack of Ila from a mubainah, i.e., a woman he divorced with a final divorce, it is because Ila is only with a wife, and there is no marital relationship in a mubainah, contrary to a revocably divorced woman, where the marital relationship remains, so Ila is valid. [3] He said: 'after that' i.e., after the Ila, except if he attaches the Ila to marriage as mentioned in suspended divorce, and if he first makes it then divorces her with a final divorce, then if its period passes while she is in iddah, she becomes separated by another, otherwise not. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah and others.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage states a key principle: Ila (the vow of abstinence) is only valid with a current wife. It does not apply to a woman who is divorced with a final (ba'in) divorce because the marriage tie is severed. In contrast, a woman divorced with a revocable (raj'i) divorce remains a wife during her iddah period, so Ila is possible. Additionally, Ila cannot be made regarding an ajnabiyah (a woman not married to him) whom he marries after making the vow, unless the vow is explicitly attached to the marriage itself (i.e., 'if I marry her, then I will abstain'). The commentator notes that if a man makes an Ila and then divorces his wife with a final divorce, if the Ila period (four months) passes while she is still in iddah, then she becomes separated from him by a final divorce (due to Ila), otherwise not. This is based on the Fatawa al-Khaniyyah.

Key Terms
المبائنة (al-mubā'inah): A woman divorced with a final (ba'in) divorce, after which the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage contract.
الأجنبية (al-ajnabiyyah): A woman who is not married to the man and is not related to him within prohibited degrees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because the marriage relationship ends with a final divorce.
C) Yes, but only if he intends to remarry her.
D) No, but only if she is in the waiting period of a revocable divorce.

Key Takeaway
Ila is only valid with a current wife; it cannot be made with a woman divorced with a final divorce or a stranger.

When a Vow of Abstinence Does Not Constitute Ila
Explains when a marital vow does not constitute ila due to indefinite exception or indirect impediment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a marital vow does not constitute ila and explain the Hanafi reasoning, including the effect of an indefinite exception.

Core Idea
A vow of abstinence that allows for intercourse within four months due to an indefinite exception is not ila.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the boundaries of ila (a vow of abstinence) by presenting two scenarios where a husband's oath does not trigger the legal consequences of ila. Understanding these exceptions is crucial because they prevent hasty applications of divorce and highlight the precision of Hanafi legal reasoning. The first scenario involves an indefinite exception ('except for a day'), while the second involves a vow that does not directly prevent intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and list the two cases where the vow is not considered ila: (1) a vow to abstain for a year except for one day, (2) a vow not to enter a city while the wife is there.
Focus on the key terms: 'except for one day' and 'By Allah I will not enter Kufa'.
2
Understand the definition of muli
Recall the Hanafi definition: a muli is one who cannot approach his wife for four months without incurring something obligatory. This definition is the benchmark for determining ila.
The husband must be unable to have intercourse within four months without violating his oath.
3
Analyze the first scenario: the indefinite exception
Examine why 'except for one day' prevents ila. The exception is indefinite (munkar), so it applies to any day. Thus, the husband can choose a day within the first four months to have intercourse, fulfilling his oath without penalty.
Imam Zufar's disagreement is noted: he interprets the exception as the last day. The Hanafi response: no evidence to support this restriction.
4
Analyze the second scenario: indirect prevention
Consider the vow not to enter Kufa while the wife is there. The husband's oath does not directly prohibit intercourse; he can have relations if she leaves Kufa. Therefore, he is not prevented for four months without obligation.
This shows that ila requires a direct and continuous prohibition of intercourse.
5
Apply the principle to borderline cases
The passage also discusses a vow with the phrase 'except a deficit of a day' – this does constitute ila because 'deficit' customarily means the end, making the abstinence continuous. This illustrates the importance of linguistic custom (urf) in Hanafi usul.
The distinction between 'except for a day' and 'except a deficit of a day' is subtle but legally significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً، وقولُهُ بالبصرة : والله لا أدخل الكوفة، وامرأته بها. ولا إيلاء من مبائنة وأجنبية نكحها بعد ذلك
قوله : وقوله؛ هذا مع ما يقارنه معطوف على قوله : «أي» بخلاف قوله : والله لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً»، وبخلاف قوله : «بالبصرة» مثلاً وامرأته بالكوفة» : «والله لا أدخل الكوفة فلا إيلاء في هاتين الصورتين أيضاً.
أما في المسألة الأولى فلأنه استثنى يوماً منكراً فيصدق على كل يوم من أيام السنة حقيقة، فيمكنه قربانها قبل مضي أربعة أشهر من غير شيء يلزمه، والمولى من لا يمكنه القربان أربعة أشهر لا بشيء يلزمه، وفيه خلاف زفر ، فإنه يصرف الاستثناء إلى الأخير، فتتم مدة المنع. وجوابه : إنه لا دليل عليه ؛ لكون المستثنى منكراً ، نعم لو قال : إلا نقصان يوم يكون مولياً ؛ لأنّ النقصان عرفاً لا يكون إلا من الآخر. كذا في «البحر ) وأما في المسألة الثانية ؛ فلأنه يمكنه وطؤها في المدة بخروجها من الكوفة، فلم يكن قوله امتناعاً عن الوطء.
قوله : ولا إيلاء... الخ ؛ أما عدم الإيلاء من مبانته ؛ أي التي طلقها طلاقاً بائناً ؛ فلأن الإيلاء إنما يكون مع الزوجة، ولا زوجية في المبانة، بخلاف مطلقة الرجعي، فإنّ الزوجية هناك قائمة، فيصح الإيلاء.
قوله : بعد ذلك ؛ أي بعد الإيلاء ، إلا إذا أضاف الإيلاء إلى الملكِ كما مرّ في الطلاق المعلق، ولو الى أولاً ثمّ أبانها، فإن مضت مدته وهي في العدة بانت بأخرى ، وإلا لا. كذا في «الفتاوى الخانية»، وغيرها.
(۱) وجه أن لا يكون مولياً أنه يمكن له قربانها في أي يوم من أيام السنة ؛ لأنه استثنى يوم منكر.
ينظر : رمز الحقائق (۱ : ۲۰۲).
(۲) «البحر الرائق» (٤ : ٧٠ - ٧١).
And (if he says) By Allah I will not approach you for a year except for a day, and his statement in Basra: By Allah I will not enter Kufa, and his wife is there. And there is no ila from an irrevocably divorced woman or a foreign woman he marries after that. His saying: 'his statement'; this with what accompanies it is coordinated with his saying: 'i.e.' contrary to his saying: By Allah I will not approach you for a year except for a day, and contrary to his saying: 'in Basra' for example and his wife is in Kufa: 'By Allah I will not enter Kufa' so there is no ila in these two cases either. As for the first case, because he excepted an indefinite day, so it applies to every day of the year in reality, and he can approach her before four months pass without anything obliging him, and the muli is one who cannot approach for four months without anything obliging him. Regarding it there is a difference of opinion from Zufar, for he directs the exception to the last, so the period of prohibition is complete. The response to it: There is no evidence for it because the excepted day is indefinite. Yes, if he said: except a deficit of a day, he is a muli because deficit customarily is only from the end. As in al-Bahr. As for the second case, because he can have intercourse with her in the period by her leaving Kufa, so his statement was not a prohibition of intercourse. His saying: 'And there is no ila...' etc.; as for the non-ilaa from an irrevocably divorced woman (mubannah), i.e. one whom he divorced irrevocably, because ila is only with the wife, and there is no marital tie in the irrevocably divorced woman, unlike a revocably divorced woman, for the marital tie exists there, so ila is valid. His saying: 'after that'; i.e. after the ila, unless he attached the ila to ownership as mentioned in suspended divorce, and if he made ila first then divorced her irrevocably, if the period passes while she is in the waiting period, she becomes divorced by another, otherwise not. As in al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya and others. (1) The reason that he is not a muli is that he can approach her on any day of the year because he excepted an indefinite day. See: Ramz al-Haqaiq (1:202). (2) Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (4:70-71).
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. It discusses two situations where a husband's vow does not constitute ila (a vow of abstinence from marital relations). First, if a husband says 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except for one day' — he is not a muli because the exception of an indefinite day means he can potentially approach her within four months (the maximum period for ila) without incurring any obligation. The commentator notes that Imam Zufar differed, interpreting 'except for one day' as referring to the last day of the year, thus making the abstinence continuous. The Hanafi response is that there is no evidence for Zufar's interpretation since the excepted day is indefinite. If he had said 'except a deficit of a day' (i.e. a year minus a day), then he would be a muli because 'deficit' customarily refers to the end. Second, if a husband in Basra says 'By Allah, I will not enter Kufa' while his wife is in Kufa, this is not ila because he can have intercourse if she leaves Kufa; his vow does not directly prevent intercourse. The passage also clarifies that ila is not valid from a woman already irrevocably divorced (mubanna) because no marital relationship remains, unlike a revocably divorced woman. If a man makes ila and then divorces irrevocably, the ruling depends on whether the waiting period ends within the ila period. The authority is from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya.

Key Terms
إيلاء (īlā'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period exceeding four months, triggering legal consequences if he does not resume within that period.
مولٍ (mūlī): A husband who has made a valid vow of abstinence (īlā') that prevents him from approaching his wife for four months without incurring something obligatory.
استثناء (istithnā'): An exception made in an oath, excluding a specific time or action from the vow's scope.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except for one day.' According to the Hanafi school, is this a valid ila?
A) Yes, because the exception is to the last day.
B) No, because the exception is indefinite, allowing intercourse within four months.
C) Yes, because any vow over four months is ila.
D) No, because ila requires no exception.

Key Takeaway
A vow of abstinence is not ila if the husband can potentially have intercourse within four months without violating his oath.

Revocable Divorcée and Conditions of Inability to Fulfill Ila
Conditions of inability to fulfill ila and status of revocable divorcée

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that constitute genuine inability ('ajz) in ila and apply them to determine whether the husband's obligation to fulfill is suspended.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman remains a wife for ila; genuine inability suspends the obligation, but legal impediments caused by choice do not.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya clarifies two important points regarding the vow of abstinence (ila). First, the husband can make ila from his revocably divorced wife because the marriage continues during the waiting period. Second, the obligation to resume intercourse (fay') within four months is suspended only if the husband is genuinely unable to do so due to specific physical or circumstantial impediments. The passage distinguishes between genuine inability and legal inability (like ihram) that the husband may have caused, emphasizing that the latter does not excuse fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on revocable divorcée
State that a revocably divorced woman is treated like a wife for ila, meaning the vow is valid and the husband must still fulfill or face consequences.
The marital bond remains during the waiting period.
2
List the conditions of genuine inability
Recall the four conditions mentioned: sickness of either spouse, wife being too young for intercourse, wife having ratq (vaginal blockage), or a distance of four months' travel between them.
These are physical or circumstantial impediments beyond the husband's control.
3
Distinguish genuine inability from legal inability
Understand that legal impediments like ihram do not excuse fulfillment if the cause is within the husband's choice. The husband is considered able in reality but unable in legal judgment.
If the husband voluntarily enters ihram, he is sinful to break it but must still fulfill by intercourse if possible.
4
Apply the continuity requirement
The inability must persist continuously from the time of ila to the end of the four-month period. If the impediment is removed, the obligation revives.
Temporary inability does not extend the period indefinitely.
5
Handle conditional ila
If the ila was conditional, the ability is assessed at the time the condition occurs, not when the vow was made.
This ensures fairness if the condition was not initially present.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses three rulings. First, a revocably divorced woman (muttalliqa raj'iyya) is treated like a wife in the context of ila, meaning that if a husband makes ila from her, it is valid because the marital bond remains during the waiting period. Second, the passage lists conditions that cause genuine inability (ajz) to fulfill ila by intercourse: sickness of either spouse, the wife being too young for intercourse, the wife having ratq (vaginal blockage), or a distance of four months' travel between them. His fulfillment (fay') must still be by actual intercourse, even if it involves sin. Importantly, this inability must persist from the time of ila to the end of the period. If the ila is conditional, the ability is assessed at the time the condition occurs. Third, the passage defines technical terms like ratq and emphasizes that the husband's minority prevents ila altogether. The authority is drawn from al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Fath, al-Misbah al-Munir, and al-Bada'i'.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): The act of resuming sexual relations with the wife, which fulfills the vow of ila and prevents its legal consequences.
رتق (ratq): A medical condition in a woman where the vaginal opening is blocked, preventing intercourse.
عجز ('ajz): Inability to perform the required act (here, intercourse) due to a genuine impediment, differentiating between legal and actual inability.

Ila' with Revocably Divorced Wife and Verbal Fay' upon Inability
Ruling on revocably divorced wife and exceptions for verbal fay'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on Ila' for a revocably divorced woman and identify the valid excuses that allow verbal fay'.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman is still treated as a wife for Ila'; if genuine inability prevents intercourse, verbal fay' suffices.

This passage addresses two interconnected rulings: first, that a revocably divorced woman (muttallaqa raj'iyya) is legally equivalent to a wife for the purpose of Ila' (a vow of abstinence), because the marriage tie persists during her 'idda. Second, it outlines the exceptional circumstances under which a husband who is genuinely unable to consummate the fay' (return to conjugal relations) may fulfill it verbally. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the law of Ila' and avoiding unjust compulsion of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status of the revocably divorced woman
The text begins by stating that she is 'like a wife' (فكالزوجة) for Ila'. This means the vow of abstinence is valid and binding, and the same four-month period applies.
This contrasts with an irrevocably divorced woman (bā'in), who is not subject to Ila' because the marriage is fully ended.
2
Recognize the normal method of fay'
The default requirement to break the vow is actual sexual intercourse. The passage then introduces exceptions where the husband is unable to do so due to genuine physical or situational obstacles.
3
List the acceptable excuses for inability
The text mentions: illness of either spouse, the wife being too young for intercourse, a physical obstruction (ratq) in the wife, or a geographical distance of four months or more between them.
The commentary clarifies that these excuses must be real (haqīqa), not merely legal impediments like ihram.
4
Understand the requirement of continuous inability
The inability must persist from the time of the vow until the end of the four-month period. If the husband becomes able before the period ends, he must perform fay' by intercourse.
If the vow was conditional, the ability is assessed at the time the condition is fulfilled, not at the time of the vow.
5
Distinguish genuine from legal inability
The commentary stresses that if the obstacle is merely legal (e.g., both spouses in ihram), the husband is considered able in reality (qādir ḥaqīqatan) and verbally unable only legally (ʿājiz ḥukman). In such cases, verbal fay' is invalid; he must have intercourse, even if it is sinful.
This distinction rests on the principle that the intent is to harm the wife; legal obstacles chosen by the husband do not excuse him.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on Ila' (vow of abstinence) in the context of a revocably divorced woman (muttallaqa raj'iyya). The author states that such a woman is treated like a wife for the purposes of Ila', because the marriage tie remains during the 'idda. Then the text addresses the case where the husband is unable to perform the fay' (return to conjugal relations) by actual intercourse due to valid reasons: illness of either spouse, the wife being too young to engage in intercourse, a physical obstruction (ratq) in the wife, or a distance of four months or more between them. If the husband later becomes able before the period ends, he must actually have intercourse. The text also notes that the wife's minority prevents valid Ila' from the start, while the husband's minority prevents the vow itself. This ruling is based on the principle that the husband's aim is to harm the wife by depriving her of sexual rights; if he is genuinely unable, verbal reconciliation suffices.

Key Terms
إيلاء (īlā'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period of four months or more.
رجعية (raj'iyya): A revocably divorced woman who is still in her 'idda period, during which the husband can take her back without a new contract.
فيء (fay'): Return to marital relations to break the vow of Ila', either by actual intercourse or, in cases of inability, by verbal declaration.

Verbal Fay' for the Incapable Husband in Ila'
Ruling on the wording of fay' and its effect when the husband is genuinely unable to have intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on verbal fay' for a husband who is genuinely unable to have intercourse, and distinguish when actual intercourse is required.

Core Idea
Verbal fay' suffices only when the husband is genuinely unable to perform intercourse; otherwise, actual intercourse is required.

In Ila', the husband's return (fay') normally requires actual intercourse to end his vow of abstinence. However, if he is truly incapable—due to illness, imprisonment, or distance—the Hanafi school permits a verbal expression of return as a dispensation. This lesson examines the conditions and limits of that dispensation as explained in the text from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognize that the passage discusses fay' (return) in the context of Ila' (vow of abstinence). Normally, fay' is accomplished by intercourse, but the text introduces an exception for the incapable husband.
Ila' is a vow by a husband to abstain from intercourse for four months.
2
State the general rule
Under normal circumstances, fay' must be actual intercourse. The wife's right to intercourse cannot be overridden by mere words.
This is the default ruling.
3
Identify the exception and its condition
The exception is for the husband who is 'ajiz (genuinely incapable) due to illness, imprisonment, distance, etc. For him, verbal fay' suffices. The condition is that his inability is genuine and ongoing at the time of the verbal statement.
The text uses the phrase 'wa huwa 'ajiz' to indicate this condition.
4
Determine the consequence
If the husband, while incapable, utters words of return (e.g., 'fittu ilayha') and the four-month period ends, the wife is not divorced. The verbal fay' is valid and effective.
The text says 'fala tulliqu ba‘dahu' — she is not divorced after that.
5
Apply the boundary condition
If the husband recovers his ability before the period ends, verbal fay' is no longer sufficient; he must have intercourse. The dispensation ends when the cause (inability) ceases.
The text says 'fa'in şahha qabla muddatihi fay'uhu biwat’ihi' — if he recovers before his period, his fay' is by intercourse.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the wording of fay' (return) in Ila'. The author states that the husband's fay' is accomplished by saying 'I return to you' (fittu ilayha). If he remains genuinely incapable throughout the four-month period and says this, his wife is not divorced. However, if he becomes able before the period ends, he must actually have intercourse. The commentary explains that verbal fay' is only valid for someone with a genuine physical inability; otherwise, intercourse is required. The reason is that the husband's sin was in verbally harming the wife by vowing abstinence, so verbal reconciliation suffices. If his ability returns, the necessity for verbal fay' ends and he must perform actual intercourse. The phrase 'fittu' and similar expressions like 'I have taken you back' or 'I have annulled the Ila'' are acceptable.

Fay’ in Ila’: Intercourse Required, Except for the Incapable
Explaining the requirement of intercourse for fay' and exception for the incapable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the requirement of intercourse for fay’ in ila’ and identify the exception for the truly incapable husband, applying the Hanafi principle of istihsan.

Core Idea
Fay’ requires intercourse; verbal fay’ suffices only if the husband is truly incapable — necessity lifts the default rule.

In the Hanafi school, when a husband makes ila’ (swearing to abstain from intercourse for four months), he must perform fay’ (return) by actual intercourse to avoid automatic divorce. But what if he is physically incapable? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the exception: the incapable husband may use verbal fay’. Let's examine the ruling and its underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
What does the passage say is required for fay’? Look for the statement that intercourse is necessary for fay’.
The default is actual intercourse.
2
Identify the exception
Find the condition that exempts a husband from the intercourse requirement. Who qualifies?
The truly incapable (عاجز عجزاً حقيقياً).
3
Understand the legal reasoning (illa)
Why does verbal fay’ suffice for the incapable? The passage explains that since the husband’s intention was not to harm by denying a right (because the wife has no right during his incapacity), but only to annoy by speech, speech-annoyance is removed by speech.
This is based on istihsan: necessity permits a substitute.
4
Note the condition for capability
What happens if the husband recovers before the four months expire? The passage states that his fay’ must then be by intercourse because the necessity for verbal fay’ ceases.
The exception is temporary; it lasts only as long as the incapacity.
5
Recognize the mode of reasoning
The passage uses istihsan (juristic preference) to allow verbal fay’ for the incapable. Identify the underlying principle: necessity (darura) permits departure from the general rule.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) not a textual one.

[1] قوله : ففيؤه ؛ مبتدأ خبره ما بعده، وحاصله : أنه لا بد في الفيء من الوطء إلا للعاجز عنه عجزاً حقيقياً، فإنّ التكلّم بما يدلّ على الرجوع نحو: فئت إليها كاف؛ لأنه آذاها بذكر المنع ، فيكون إرضاؤها بالوعد باللسان، فإنه لَمَّا كان عاجزاً عن الوطء حالة الإيلاء لم يكن قصده الإضرار بمنع حقها في الجماع ؛ إذ لا حق لها حالة العجز، وإنما قصده إيحاشها باللسان، ويرتفع ذلك باللسان. كذا في «البناية»). [٢]قوله : فئت؛ بصيغة المتكلّم من الفيء بمعنى الرجوع، وكذا : راجعتك وأبطلت الإيلاء ونحو ذلك. [٣] قوله : فإن صح ؛ أي المريض والأولى أن يقول : فإن قدر ؛ ليشمل المريض [٤] قوله : بوطئه ؛ لأنَّ إقامة الفيء اللساني مقام الوطء كانت للضرورة، فمتى ارتفعت ارتفعت.
His statement: 'I return to her' is his fay'; then she is not divorced after that if his term passes while he is incapable, and if he becomes healthy before his term, then his fay' is by intercourse. [1] His statement: 'his fay'': a subject whose predicate follows; its summary: that in fay', intercourse is necessary except for one who is truly incapable of it; then speaking what indicates return such as 'I return to her' suffices, because he harmed her by mentioning the prohibition, so pleasing her by promise with the tongue suffices; since at the time of ila' he was incapable of intercourse, his intention was not to harm by preventing her right to coition—for she has no right during incapacity—rather his intention was to annoy her by speech, and that is removed by speech. Thus in al-Binaya. [2] His statement: 'I return': in the first person from fay' meaning return, likewise 'I take you back', 'I nullify the ila'', and similar. [3] His statement: 'if he becomes healthy': i.e., the sick one; it would be better to say 'if he is able' to include the sick. [4] His statement: 'by his intercourse': because the substitution of verbal fay' for intercourse was due to necessity; when it is removed, it is removed.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from the commentator on the matn of al-Wiqayah. The ruling: In the case of ila' (a vow to abstain from intercourse), the husband must perform fay' by actual intercourse to avoid automatic divorce after four months. However, if the husband is truly incapable of intercourse (e.g., due to illness), then verbal fay'—saying 'I return to you' or similar—suffices. The commentator clarifies the reasoning: since the incapable husband did not intend harm by denying her right (as she has no right during incapacity), but only annoyed her by speech, speech suffices to end the ila'. If he recovers before the expiry of the four months, his fay' must be by intercourse because the necessity for verbal fay' ceases. The commentator also notes that the phrase 'if he becomes healthy' could be better phrased as 'if he is able' to include other cases.

Key Terms
الفيء (al-fay'): Return to intercourse after ila', or verbal declaration of return for the incapable.
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): A vow by the husband to abstain from intercourse with his wife for four months or longer.
عاجز عجزاً حقيقياً (ʿājiz ʿajzan ḥaqīqiyyan): Truly incapable of intercourse due to physical or mental condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the required form of fay' for a husband in ila' who is physically capable of intercourse?
A) Saying 'I return to you'
B) Intercourse
C) Any kind of physical contact
D) Paying a penalty

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fay' in ila' requires intercourse, but the truly incapable husband may use verbal fay' by istihsan, and if capability returns before the term, intercourse becomes required again.

The Hanafi Position on 'You Are Forbidden to Me': Intention Determines the Ruling
Ruling on the phrase 'you are forbidden' based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three-case Hanafi ruling for the phrase 'Anti 'alayya haram' based on intention and explain why the school chose Ibn Mas'ud's view.

Core Idea
The phrase 'you are forbidden' yields divorce, zihar, or ila' according to what the husband intended.

The Companions differed on the legal effect of saying 'haram' to one's wife. The Hanafi school, following Ibn Mas'ud, holds that the speaker's intention is decisive. This lesson unpacks that position and the three possible outcomes: divorce, zihar, or ila'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sahaba ikhtilaf
Note the three positions: some said it is an oath, some said three divorces, and Ibn Mas'ud said it depends on intention.
The source gives specific narrations from Abu Bakr, Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Ali, and Zayd.
2
Recognize the Hanafi adoption
The author states that the Hanafi school chose Ibn Mas'ud's view as the strongest.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Memorize the three-case rule
If he intends divorce, it is one bain divorce. If he intends zihar, three divorces, or falsehood, it is what he intended. If he intends mere prohibition without specification, it is ila'.
The text lists these sequentially after the ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the proof strength
The proof is the Companion saying of Ibn Mas'ud, which is considered a strong textual evidence.
The argument type is dalil naqli.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت علي حرام) [0] قوله : وأنت علي حرام ؛ اختلفت الصحابة فيه على ما فصله الحافظ ابنُ حجر في «تلخيص الحبير»: «فأخرج ابن أبي شيبة عن أبي بكر وعمر وابن مسعود أنهم قالوا : مَن قال لامرأته : هي علي حرام فليست بحرام، وعليه كفارة اليمين ونحوه ، أخرجه البيهقي والدارقطني. وروى البيهقي عن علي وزيد أنها ثلاث تطليقات». وأخرج البيهقي عن ابن مسعود من طرق أنه قال: «نيته في الحرام ما نوى، إن لم يكن نوى طلاقاً فيمين»(۲) (۳). وهذا هو الذي اختاره أصحابنا، وهو أقوى المذاهب. [1] إن نَوَى به الطلاق، فبائنة [۲] ، وإن نوى به الظهار، أو الثلاث، أو الكذب، فما نوى [۳] ، وإن نوى التحريم ولم ينو شيئاً فإيلاء [٤]. [0] قوله : للعرف ; يعني أن لفظ التحريم خاصاً كان أو عاماً نحو : أنت علي حرام، أو : كلّ حلال علي حرام، أو : كلّ حلال الله أو حلال المسلمين علي حرام، يستعمل في العرف للطلاق، فيقع الطلاق به بلا نَيّة، وستطلع على تفصيله في بحث الإيمان. وقيل : هو وكل حل علي حرام، وهرجه بدست راست كيرم بروي حرام، طلاق بلا نية للعرف، وبه يفتى
(And 'You are forbidden to me') [0] His statement: 'You are forbidden to me'; the Companions differed about it as detailed by al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar in Talkhis al-Habir: 'Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated from Abu Bakr, Umar, and Ibn Mas'ud that they said: Whoever says to his wife 'You are forbidden to me' it is not forbidden, and he owes the expiation of an oath and similar; narrated by al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni. And al-Bayhaqi narrated from Ali and Zayd that it is three divorces.' And al-Bayhaqi narrated from Ibn Mas'ud through multiple chains that he said: 'His intention in the word 'haram' is what he intended; if he did not intend divorce, then it is an oath.' This is what our scholars chose, and it is the strongest of the schools. [1] If he intends divorce by it, then it is a bain [divorce]; [2] and if he intends zihar, or three [divorces], or falsehood, then what he intended; [3] and if he intends prohibition and does not intend anything, then it is ila'. [0] His statement: 'due to custom'; meaning that the word of prohibition, whether specific like 'You are forbidden to me' or general like 'All that is lawful is forbidden to me' or 'All that Allah has made lawful or the Muslims have made lawful is forbidden to me', is used in custom for divorce, so divorce occurs by it without intention. And you will see its detail in the chapter on oaths. And it is said: 'Everything that is lawful to me is forbidden, and whatever I hold in my hand or keep as correct is forbidden' is divorce without intention due to custom, and that is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the phrase 'Anti 'alayya haram' (you are forbidden to me) and similar statements. The commentator first narrates the ikhtilaf among the Sahaba: some said it is not forbidden but requires expiation of an oath, some said it is three divorces, and Ibn Mas'ud said it depends on intention—if no divorce intended, it is an oath. The Hanafi school adopts Ibn Mas'ud's view as strongest, but adds that by prevalent custom, this phrase is used for divorce, so it takes effect as one bain divorce without intention. The text then details the ruling based on intention: if he intends divorce, it is one bain divorce; if he intends zihar, three divorces, or falsehood, it is what he intended; if he intends mere prohibition without specification, it is ila'. Finally, the commentator explains that custom ('urf) is the basis for the fatwa that divorce occurs without intention when using such phrases, because people use them for divorce. This is the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms
الحرام (al-haram): A phrase of prohibition; its legal effect depends on intention and custom.
بائنة (ba'inah): A final (irrevocable) divorce that ends the marriage, but without triple effect.
ظهار (zihar): An oath comparing the wife to a female relative, requiring expiation before resuming marital relations.
إيلاء (ila'): A vow to abstain from marital relations for four months, leading to divorce if not resumed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi adoption of Ibn Mas'ud, if a husband intends mere prohibition (not divorce, zihar, etc.) when he says 'Anti 'alayya haram', what is the ruling?
A) It is an oath requiring expiation
B) It is one bain divorce
C) It is ila'
D) It is three divorces

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the legal effect of saying 'haram' to your wife depends on what you intended: divorce, zihar, or ila'.

The Role of Custom ('Urf) in the Fatwa: Divorce without Intention for 'You Are Forbidden'
Explanation of custom's role in effecting divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why custom overrides the need for intention in the later Hanafi fatwa and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Due to prevalent custom, saying 'you are forbidden' counts as divorce even without intention.

While the base Hanafi position gives weight to intention, the later fatwa accepts that if the phrase is commonly used for divorce in society, it takes effect as divorce without any intention. This lesson explores the custom argument and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base rule
Recall that the default is intention governs: if no intention, it is an oath (Ibn Mas'ud).
This sets the stage for the exception.
2
Identify the custom exception
The commentator states that due to custom ('urf), the phrase is used for divorce, so divorce occurs without intention.
The phrase 'به يفتى' indicates this is the operative fatwa.
3
Apply the fatwa to examples
The text gives examples: 'كل حل علي حرام' and a Persian phrase. These are all considered divorce without intention because of custom.
Custom here means the common usage of the community.
4
Understand the authority weight
This fatwa is from the later Hanafi scholars (fatawa mutakhkhirin), overriding earlier positions.
It is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

The Effect of 'Anti Alayya Haram': Intention and Custom
Ruling on intent in ila' and fatwa by custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'anti alayya haram' with and without intention, and explain how custom (urf) shifts the fatwa from ila' to divorce.

Core Idea
Without specific intention, the phrase is ila' by text but divorce by custom — later fatwa prevails.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya examines a common but legally complex expression: when a husband says 'you are haram upon me,' what is the legal effect? The answer depends on his intention, but when no intention is declared, the ruling is influenced by prevailing custom. The text highlights a shift from the earlier ruling of ila' to the later fatwa of divorce, demonstrating how ‘urf can override literal meaning in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and possible intentions
Recognize that the phrase 'anti alayya haram' can be intended as divorce, zihar, three divorces, lying, or simple prohibition. Each intention triggers a different ruling.
The passage mentions these five possibilities.
2
Determine the ruling for each intention
For divorce intent: one ba'in divorce. For zihar or three divorces: as intended. For lying: religiously believed but not judicially; custom overrides. For prohibition or no intent: the matn says ila', but the mufta bihi says divorce due to custom.
The commentator notes a nuance about lying.
3
Understand the role of custom
Later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin) relied on the prevalent custom that such phrases are commonly used to effect divorce. Hence, even without explicit intention, the phrase causes a single ba'in divorce.
The dalil is 'urf (custom), which is a valid source when no explicit text contradicts.
4
Distinguish between the earlier position and the mufta bihi
The matn (earlier position) holds that without intention it is ila'. The later fatwa (mufta bihi) holds it is divorce. The tarjih is based on custom.
This distinction is critical for proper fatwa application.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a husband says 'anti alayya haram' without any intention, the mufta bihi is that one ba'in divorce occurs. This is the operative ruling in Hanafi courts today.
The concept of 'urf al-‘amal (customary practice) is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الإيلاء إن نَوَى به الطلاق، فبائنة ، وإن نوى به الظهار، أو الثلاث، أو الكذب، فما نوى، وإن نوى التحريم ولم ينو شيئاً فإيلاء. [١] قوله : فبائنة ؛ أي واحدة ؛ لكونها المتيقن إلا أن ينوي الثلاث، ولا تصح فيه نية الثنتين لما مر في موضعه. [٢] قوله : فما نوى ؛ يعني الطلقات الثلاث أو الظهار، وإن كان غرضه الكذب يصدق ديانة ولا يصدق قضاء، بل يكون إيلاء أو طلاقـاً للعــرف الحادث. كذا في «الفتح» [۳] قوله : فما نوى ؛ فيه مسامحة ظاهرة، فإنّ الكذب ليس من معاني اللفظ ، فالأولى أن يفرد الكذب بالذكر، ويقال : هو هدر إن نوى الكذب. [٤] قوله : فإيلاء ؛ أما في صورة نيّة التحريم فظاهر، وأما في صورة عدم النية فلان هذا اللفظ بصيغة دال على التحريم فيكون إيلاء. [0]قوله : للعرف ؛ يعني أن لفظ التحريم خاصاً كان أو عاماً نحو : أنت علي حرام، أو : كلّ حلال علي حرام، أو : كلّ حلال الله أو حلال المسلمين علي حرام، يستعمل في العرف للطلاق، فيقع الطلاق به بلا نَيّة، وستطلع على تفصيله في بحث الإيمان.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Ila'. If he intended by it divorce, then it is a ba'in divorce. If he intended by it zihar, or three [divorces], or lying, then it is what he intended. If he intended prohibition and did not intend anything, then it is ila'. [1] His statement: 'then it is ba'in' – meaning one [ba'in divorce]; because it is the certain one unless he intends three, and the intention of two is not valid due to what has passed in its place. [2] His statement: 'then it is what he intended' – meaning the three divorces or zihar, and if his purpose was lying, he is believed religiously but not judicially; rather it becomes ila' or divorce due to the emergent custom. Thus in al-Fath. [3] His statement: 'then it is what he intended' – there is an apparent inaccuracy in it, for lying is not among the meanings of the phrase; so it is better to mention lying separately and say: it is null if he intended lying. [4] His statement: 'then it is ila'' – as for the case of intending prohibition, it is apparent; as for the case of no intention, because this phrase is a form indicating prohibition, so it is ila'. [0] His statement: 'for custom' – means that the word of prohibition, whether specific or general like 'you are haram upon me' or 'every halal is upon me haram' or 'every halal of Allah or the Muslims is upon me haram', is used in custom for divorce, so divorce occurs by it without intention, and you will come across its detail in the discussion of oaths.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the rulings of ila' expressions. The matn states that if a husband says 'you are haram upon me' and intends divorce, it becomes one ba'in divorce (the certain minimum). If he intends zihar or three divorces, it takes effect as intended. If he intends lying about the entire statement, he is believed religiously but not in court, and custom (which has changed to treat these words as divorce) makes it divorce or ila'. The commentary clarifies: the word 'ba'in' means one ba'in divorce. The intention of two divorces is invalid as per earlier discussion. When he intends lying, the ruling is that it is null (not effective) if he is truthful in intention, but judicially custom prevails. The commentator notes a subtlety: lying is not a meaning of the phrase itself, so the author should have mentioned it separately. Finally, if he intends prohibition or has no specific intent, the phrase is treated as ila' because it literally indicates prohibition. However, the fatwa later (based on 'urf) is that it counts as divorce without intention due to prevalent custom. Thus, the relied-upon position is that such expressions result in divorce by custom, not ila'.

Key Terms
إيلاء (īlā'): A vow by the husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for four months or more.
ظهار (ẓihār): A declaration likening the wife to a prohibited relative (e.g., mother), requiring expiation before resuming relations.
عرف (‘urf): Established custom or common usage recognized by the community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa) ruling when a husband says 'anti alayya haram' with no particular intention?
A) It is considered ila'
B) It is considered one ba'in divorce
C) It is considered zihar
D) It is considered meaningless without intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, custom can override the literal meaning of a phrase: the expression 'anti alayya haram' without intention is treated as a ba'in divorce, not ila'.

Khul': Definition and Ruling
Definition of khul' and its permissibility when needed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khul' according to the Hanafi school, state its ruling (mubah only when needed), and identify the Quranic proof with its condition.

Core Idea
Khul' is permissible only when spouses cannot maintain Allah's limits; without need, it is not allowed.

This lesson defines khul' — a divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation. The Hanafi school emphasizes that khul' is not a general permission but is restricted to cases of genuine marital discord. We will examine the definition, the condition of need, and the Quranic basis.

Guided Path
1
Define the term khul'
Start with the linguistic meaning (removal) and then the legal definition: removal of marital authority contingent on the wife's acceptance using the word 'khul' or its meaning.
The text distinguishes between a simple divorce pronouncement and true khul'.
2
Identify the ruling and its condition
The matn says 'لا بأس به عند الحاجة' (no harm when there is need). The commentary clarifies that without need, it is not permissible. Determine the default ruling (makruh/haram?) and the conditional permissibility.
The condition is genuine discord where reconciliation is impossible.
3
Examine the Quranic proof (dalil)
Surah al-Baqarah 2:229 is cited. The exception clause 'إِلَّا أَن يَخَافَا أَلَّا يُقِيمَا حُدُودَ اللَّهِ' provides the condition. Analyze how this verse supports the ruling.
The verse explicitly ties permissibility to fear of not maintaining Allah's limits.
4
Distinguish khul' from other forms of divorce
If the husband says 'khala'tuka' and intends divorce, it is a regular talaq ba'in, not khul'. If he divorces her for payment, it is not khul' that drops the mahr. Understand the differences.
Khul' requires a specific contingent offer and acceptance.
5
Apply the condition to a hypothetical scenario
Given a scenario, determine if khul' is permissible based on the presence or absence of genuine need.

باب الخلع لا بأس به عند الحاجة [١]قوله : باب الخلع ؛ إنّما أخَّره عن الإيلاء مع أنّ المناسب تقديمه عليه ؛ لكونه طلاقاً دون الإيلاء ؛ لأنّ الإيلاء قد يكون طلاقاً وهو بلا عوض، والخلع طلاق بالعوض، فكان الإيلاء أقرب إلى الطلاق، وأيضاً الإيلاء تعد ونشوز من الرجل والخُلعُ نشوز من المرأة ، فكان الخلع أحق بالتأخير. كذا في «العناية». والخلع بالفتح : النزع ، يقال : خَلعَ ثوبه عن بدنه ؛ أي نزع، وبالضم اسم، يقال : خالعت المرأة خلعاً : إذ افتدت منه بمالها. كذا في «الكفاية». وعرفوه شرعاً على ما في «الفتح» وغيره : بأنه إزالة ملك النكاح المتوقفة على قبولها بلفظ : الخلع أو ما في معناه، فلو قال : خلعتك، ونوى به الطلاق يقع واحداً بائناً، ولا يكون خلعاً ؛ لعدم توقفه على قبولها ، ولو طلقها على مال لا يكون خلعاً مسقطاً للمهر. والأصل فيه قوله : ﴿ الطَّلَاقُ مَرَّتَانِ فَإِمْسَاكُ بِمَعْرُوفٍ أَوْ تَسْرِيحُ بِإِحْسَنِ وَلَا يَحِلُّ لَكُمْ أَن تَأْخُذُوا مِمَّا ءَاتَيْتُمُوهُنَّ شَيْئًا إِلَّا أَن يَخَافَا أَلَّا يُقِيمَا حُدُودَ اللَّهِ فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلا يُقِيمَا حُدُودَ اللَّهِ فلا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا فِيمَا افْتَدَتْ بِهِ [٢]قوله : لا بأس به ؛ أي بالخلع عند وقوع الحاجة)، وهي وقوع التخالف بين الزوجين بحيث أن لا يرجى التوافق وحسن المعاشرة، وأشار به إلى أنّ عند عدم الحاجة لا يجوز، وعند الحاجة أيضاً الأولى الاحتراز عنه مهما أمكن.
Chapter of Khul'. There is no harm in it when there is need. [1] His statement: 'Chapter of Khul'. He delayed it after Ila' despite the appropriateness of presenting it before it, because it is divorce while Ila' is not; for Ila' may be divorce and it is without compensation, while Khul' is divorce with compensation, so Ila' is closer to divorce. Also, Ila' is transgression and nushuz from the man, while Khul' is nushuz from the woman, so Khul' is more entitled to be delayed. Thus in al-'Inayah. And Khul' with fath [vowel] means removal (naz') – one says khala'a thawbahu 'an badanihi, i.e., removed it; and with damm it is a noun – one says khal'atu al-mar'ata khul'an: when she ransoms herself from him with her wealth. Thus in al-Kifayah. And they defined it legally according to al-Fath and others: that it is the removal of marital authority that is contingent upon her acceptance using the word 'khul' or its meaning. So if he says 'khala'tuka' (I have divorced you) and intended divorce, one ba'in divorce falls, but it is not khul' because it is not contingent upon her acceptance; and if he divorced her for wealth, it is not khul' that drops the dower. The proof for it is His statement: 'Divorce is [revocable] twice; then honorable retention or kind release. And it is not lawful for you to take anything of what you have given them unless both fear that they will not maintain [the] limits of Allah. So if you fear that they will not maintain the limits of Allah, there is no blame upon them for that by which she ransoms herself.' [2] His statement: 'There is no harm in it' – i.e., in khul' when need arises, which is the occurrence of discord between spouses such that harmony and good companionship cannot be hoped for. And he indicated by it that when there is no need, it is not permissible, and even when there is need, it is better to avoid it as much as possible.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage provides the definition and ruling of khul'. It begins with the matn stating that khul' is permissible when there is need, meaning when marital discord makes reconciliation impossible. The commentary explains why the chapter on khul' is placed after ila': because ila' is closer to divorce (being without compensation) while khul' is with compensation, and also because ila' is transgression by the husband while khul' is transgression by the wife. The definition: khul' is the removal of marital authority contingent on the wife's acceptance, using the word 'khul' or its equivalent. If the husband simply says 'khala'tuka' and intends divorce, it becomes one ba'in divorce but not khul' because the wife's acceptance is not required. If he divorces her for a payment, it is not khul' and does not drop the dower. The Quranic proof is from Surah al-Baqarah 2:229, which permits taking compensation only when both fear not maintaining Allah's limits. The commentary emphasizes that khul' is not permissible without genuine need (discord), and even with need it is better to avoid it if possible.

Key Terms
خلع (khul‘): Removal of marital authority in exchange for compensation, contingent on the wife's acceptance.
إزالة ملك النكاح (izālat milk al-nikāḥ): Termination of the marital bond, i.e., dissolution of marriage.
افتداء (iftidā’): Ransom; the wife paying the husband to free herself from the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Permissible
B) Recommended
C) Not permissible
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
Khul' is permissible only when genuine marital discord threatens the limits of Allah; without need, it is invalid.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Khul' is an Irrevocable Divorce with Valid Dower
Explains the ruling and proof that khul' is a ba'in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that khul' is a ba'in divorce and explain the evidence from the hadith of Thabit ibn Qays and the Quranic context that supports this position.

Core Idea
Khul' is a ba'in divorce, not an annulment, because the Prophet commanded Thabit to 'divorce her' after accepting compensation.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses a key point of disagreement: is khul' (divorce initiated by the wife through compensation) a divorce or an annulment? The Hanafi school, supported by the hadith of Thabit ibn Qays and the Quranic placement of khul' within divorce verses, firmly holds that it is an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. We will examine the evidence and the practical implications of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on khul'
Identify that the Hanafi school considers khul' a valid (sahih) transaction that results in a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce, provided the compensation is valid dower.
The passage begins by clarifying the condition: 'with whatever is valid as dower' – the compensation must be property that can legally serve as mahr.
2
Identify the opposing view
The passage notes that some scholars, including Ibn Abbas and al-Shafi'i in his old opinion, considered khul' an annulment (faskh) rather than a divorce.
Al-Shafi'i later retracted this view, and the relied-upon Shafi'i position agrees with the Hanafis.
3
Examine the primary Hanafi dalil: the hadith of Thabit ibn Qays
The Prophet instructed Thabit: 'Accept the garden and divorce her with one divorce.' The imperative 'taliqha' (divorce her) explicitly indicates that the separation is a divorce.
This hadith is sahih (narrated by al-Bukhari) and is considered the clearest evidence by Hanafi usul scholars.
4
Analyze the Quranic context for khul'
Allah mentions khul' in the middle of the divorce verses (al-Baqarah 229), after stating 'Divorce is twice' (revocable) and before the third divorce (irrevocable). This placement shows that khul' is a type of divorce.
The usul scholars argue that inserting khul' within the divorce framework indicates it shares the same legal nature.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If khul' is a divorce, it counts as one of the three divorces, and the woman must observe the 'iddah of divorce. If it is an annulment, it does not count as a divorce and may have different 'iddah rules.
The Hanafi position is that khul' is a ba'in divorce, so it counts as one irrevocable divorce, reducing the husband's remaining divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما يصلح مهراً، وهو طلاق بائن
يدل عليه حديث كون الطلاق أبغض المباحات كما مر ذكره، وحديث : المختلعات هن المنافقات» (١) ، أخرجه التِّرْمِذِيّ ، وحديث : «أيما امرأة سألت زوجها الطلاق من غير بأس، فحرام عليها رائحة الجنّة»(۲)، أخرجه الترمذي. [١]قوله : بما يصلح مهراً ؛ أي بمال يصح جعله مهراً، فلا يصح بما ليس بمال ، وبما هو مال غير متقوم. [۲] قوله : طلاق بائن ؛ اختلف فيه، فقيل : إنّه فسخ وفرقةٌ من غير طلاق، وهو المروي عن ابن عباس الله ، أخرجه أحمد، وبه قال الشافعي في القديم، لكن الصحيح من مذهبه المرجوع إليه أنه طلاق، كما صرح به التفتازاني في «التلويح»، وهو المروي عن عثمان ، أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ»، وقال علي الله : «لا تكون طلقة بائنة إلا في فدية أو إيلاء» (۳) ، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، ومثله أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة عن ابن مسعود
، وقال ابن عمر : عدّتها - أي المختلعة - عدة المطلقة»، أخرجه ابنُ حزم ومالك وغيرهما.
ويشهد له حديث : «الخلع تطليقة بائنة» (۳) ، أخرجه البيهقي والدارقطني بسند ضعيف، ويدل عليه قول النبي الثابت بن قيس له . طلبت امرأته الخلع : اقبل الحديقة وطلقها تطليقه» (٤) ، أخرجه البخاري.
وكان ذلك أوّل خُلع في الإسلام ) ، كما ورد في رواية أحمد والبزار، «وكان الحديقة صداقها أعطاها ثابت له ، كما ورد في رواية أبي داود. وكون الخلع طلاقاً هو منطوق القرآن، كما فصله الأصوليون، وتفصيله : إنّ الله ذكر الطلاق المعقب للرجعة بقوله : الطَّلَقُ مَرَّتَانِ )، وذكر الثالث بقوله : فَإِن طَلَّقَهَا فَلَا تَحِلُّ لَهُ مِنْ بَعْدُ حَتَّى تَنكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَهُ )
وأدرج في أثناء ذكر الطلاق بحث الخلع حيث قال : فَإِنْ خِفْتُم ) : أي علمتم أو ظننتم أيها الحكام، ألا يقيما ) : أي الزوجان ، حُدُودَ الله : أي حقوق الزوجية، فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا ) : أي فلا إثم على الزوجين ، فيا افْتَدَتْ بِهِ ، فخص
With whatever is valid as dower, and it is an irrevocable divorce. That is indicated by the hadith that divorce is the most hateful of permissible things as mentioned earlier, and by the hadith: 'Women who seek khul' are hypocrites' narrated by al-Tirmidhi, and the hadith: 'Any woman who asks her husband for divorce without cause, the scent of Paradise is forbidden for her' narrated by al-Tirmidhi. [1] His statement: 'with whatever is valid as dower' means with wealth that is valid to be made dower; so it is not valid with what is not wealth, nor with what is wealth but non-fungible. [2] His statement: 'irrevocable divorce'; there is disagreement about it. It is said that it is annulment and separation without divorce, which is narrated from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) by Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i held this in his old opinion, but the correct view of his school that is relied upon is that it is divorce, as stated by al-Taftazani in 'al-Talwih'. It is also narrated from Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) by Malik in 'al-Muwatta', and Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'There is no irrevocable divorce except in ransom or ila'' narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah, and similar is narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah from Ibn Mas'ud. And Ibn Umar said: 'Her waiting period - meaning the woman who seeks khul' - is the waiting period of a divorced woman' narrated by Ibn Hazm, Malik, and others. Supporting this is the hadith: 'Khul' is one irrevocable divorce' narrated by al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni with a weak chain, and it is indicated by the Prophet's statement to Thabit ibn Qays when his wife sought khul': 'Accept the garden and divorce her with one divorce' narrated by al-Bukhari. That was the first khul' in Islam as narrated by Ahmad and al-Bazzar, and the garden was her dower given to her by Thabit as narrated by Abu Dawud. That khul' is a divorce is the explicit meaning of the Qur'an as elaborated by the usul scholars. Its detail: Allah mentioned the divorce that allows return (revocability) in His statement: 'Divorce is twice' (al-Baqarah 229), and mentioned the third in His statement: 'Then if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband' (al-Baqarah 230). And He inserted the topic of khul' in the midst of discussing divorce where He said: 'If you fear' i.e., know or think, O judges, 'that they (the two spouses) will not maintain the limits of Allah' i.e., the rights of marriage, 'then there is no sin upon them' i.e., upon the two spouses 'in what she ransoms herself with' (al-Baqarah 229), thus specifying it.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife through compensation). The author first clarifies that khul' is valid only with property that can serve as dower (mahr), meaning it must be mal (wealth) and must be mutaqawwim (valuable in Islamic law). It cannot be something that is not considered wealth or is non-fungible (ghayr mutaqawwim). Then, the author addresses the disputed nature of khul': is it a divorce (talaq) or an annulment (faskh)? The Hanafi position, supported by many Companions, is that it is a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. The passage presents evidence from hadith and Quran. The hadith of Thabit ibn Qays, where the Prophet instructed him to accept the garden (the dower) and divorce her with one divorce, is taken as a clear indication that khul' is a divorce. The Quranic verses on divorce (al-Baqarah 229-230) treat khul' within the context of divorce, implying it is a form thereof. The author notes that some, like Ibn Abbas and the old view of al-Shafi'i, considered it an annulment, but the relied-upon view of the Shafi'i school later retracted to agree that it is a divorce. The Hanafi school firmly holds it is a ba'in divorce, and this has practical consequences for the 'iddah (waiting period) and remarriage eligibility. The passage also quotes the hadith condemning women who seek divorce without cause, emphasizing that khul' should only be sought for valid reasons such as fear of not maintaining Allah's limits.

Key Terms
خلع (khul’): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband, resulting in an irrevocable separation.
طلاق بائن (talāq bā’in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage and new dower.
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife at marriage, and in khul' it becomes the compensation the wife pays to free herself.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of marriage without pronouncing divorce, which some scholars considered khul' to be.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Revocable divorce (raj‘ī)
B) Irrevocable divorce (bā’in)
C) It is not a divorce but an annulment
D) A conditional divorce

Key Takeaway
Khul' is a valid irrevocable divorce (ba'in) in the Hanafi school, provided the compensation is sound property that can serve as dower.
