The Wife's Right to Retract and Stipulate Options in Khul'
Rules for wife's retraction and stipulation of options in khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the wife's retraction before acceptance and the validity of stipulating khiyar al-shart in khul', and apply the rule of session (majlis) continuity.

Core Idea
Khul' from the wife's side is a sale; the husband's offer is an oath—hence the dispute on options.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains key rules of khul' when initiated by the wife. It covers her right to retract before the husband accepts, the possibility of stipulating an option for her, and the requirement that acceptance occur in the same session. Most importantly, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a stipulated option is valid, with Abu Hanifa allowing it and the Sahibayn rejecting it, based on different analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's right to retract before acceptance
The passage states that if the wife initiates khul' (offer), she may retract before the husband accepts. This is analogous to a sale contract where the offeror can withdraw before acceptance. Confirm the condition that retraction is only valid before acceptance.
2
Understand the dispute on khiyar al-shart (stipulated option)
Abu Hanifa holds that the wife can stipulate an option (e.g., three days) in her favor; if she rejects within the period, no talaq occurs. The Sahibayn hold that any option is void—talaq and consideration are immediate. Note which side each imam takes.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the proofs: sale analogy vs oath analogy
Abu Hanifa analogizes khul' from the wife's side to a sale (she pays consideration), where options are valid. The Sahibayn argue that the husband's utterance is like an oath (yamin), which does not admit rescission, and her acceptance completes that oath. Understand each proof's strength.
The argument type is qiyas khafi.
4
Apply the thamarah (practical difference)
If an option is stipulated and the wife rejects within the period, Abu Hanifa says no talaq and no payment; the Sahibayn say talaq occurs and payment is due. This difference affects actual divorce outcomes.
5
Apply the majlis (session) requirement
The contract of khul' is restricted to the same session (majlis). If acceptance occurs after a delay or outside the session, it is invalid. This follows the rules of exchange contracts.

Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage explains the rules of khul' concerning the wife's right to retract, stipulating an option (khiyar) for her, and the limitation to the session (majlis). The author notes that if the wife initiates khul', she may retract before the husband accepts, analogizing to sale. Regarding khiyar, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa allows stipulating an option for the wife (e.g., three days), so that if she chooses within that period, talaq occurs and the consideration is due; if she rejects, nothing occurs. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree, holding that stipulating an option is invalid; once the husband utters the conditional talaq and she accepts, talaq falls and the consideration is obligatory regardless. Their reasoning: the husband's proposal is like an oath (yamin) in meaning—it is a conditional statement—and an oath does not admit rescission. Her acceptance completes the oath, so it is not subject to option. Abu Hanifa counters that khul' from the woman's side is akin to a sale, where options are valid. The author also notes that only khiyar al-shart (stipulated option) is possible; khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) does not apply to khul', but khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) in the consideration is recognized for serious defects. Finally, the contract is limited to the session; if acceptance occurs after a delay, it is not valid. This is in line with the rules of exchange contracts.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a contract allowing one party to ratify or rescind within a specified period.
يمين (yamin): Oath; in this context, the husband's conditional utterance of talaq is treated as an oath that is completed by the wife's acceptance.
مجلس (majlis): The session or meeting where the contract is concluded; both offer and acceptance must occur in the same sitting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No talaq occurs and no payment is due.
B) Talaq occurs and payment is due.
C) The khul' is invalid from the start.
D) Talaq occurs but no payment is due.

Key Takeaway
In khul', the wife can retract before the husband accepts, but any stipulation of a waiting option is generally invalid per the mufta bihi view of the Sahibayn; the contract must be completed in the same session.

Conditions of Khul': Retraction, Option, and Session
Explaining conditions of khul' with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid khul' from the wife's side, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on stipulation of option, and apply the dual nature of khul' to determine whether a given condition is valid.

Core Idea
Khul' is a transaction for the wife and an oath for the husband — each side has different rights.

This lesson unpacks the three key conditions of khul' (divorce by compensation) from the wife's perspective: her right to retract before acceptance, her ability to stipulate an option period, and the requirement that acceptance occur in the same session. We also explore the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on whether option stipulation is valid, a dispute rooted in the dual nature of khul'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and initiation
Note who initiates khul': if the wife initiates, she can retract before husband's acceptance; if husband initiates, he cannot retract. This asymmetry flows from the dual nature.
2
Analyze the stipulation of option
Abu Hanifa allows the wife to stipulate an option period; Sahibayn reject this. Understand their reasoning: one analogizes to sale, the other to oath.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, not inter-madhhab.
3
Determine the session rule
When wife initiates, husband's acceptance must be in the same session; if he initiates, wife may accept later in her own session. This also stems from the transaction vs. oath distinction.
The session rule for husband-initiated khul' is more flexible.
4
Apply the dual nature
Recognize that khul' from wife's side is a transaction (mu'awadah), from husband's side an oath (yamin). This governs who can retract, who can stipulate options, and the session requirement.
The maxim: 'Khul' is a transaction from her side, an oath from his side.'

And (it is confined to the session). His statement: 'until...' etc. is a branching from its being a transaction from her side. His statement: 'if the offer is from her' means if the initiation of khul' is from the wife by saying: I have ransomed myself for such-and-such, then she may retract from it as long as the husband has not accepted, according to the ruling of sale and other transactions that the offeror may retract before his offer is confirmed by the acceptance of the other. His statement: 'and stipulation' (with nominative) is a conjunction to his statement 'her retraction'; meaning the stipulation of option for her is valid, such that the husband says: I divorce you for such-and-such on condition that you have the option for three days, and she accepts, the condition is permissible; if she chooses (the divorce) within the period, talaq occurs and the money is due; if she rejects, it does not occur and nothing is due according to him. According to the two, stipulation of option is void, talaq occurs and the money is due. Their argument: option is for rescission after conclusion, not for prevention of conclusion. And the dispositions here (the husband's offer and the wife's acceptance) do not admit rescission from both sides: from his side because it is an oath, as it is a condition and a consequence in meaning, and an oath does not accept rescission; from her side because the wife's acceptance is a condition for the completion of the oath, as the husband's oath is completed by her acceptance, so her acceptance takes the ruling of the oath. His (Abu Hanifa's) argument: khul' on her side is like a sale, so stipulation of option therein is valid. Thus in al-Nihayah. And in al-Bahr: 'It is restricted to option of stipulation because option of inspection is not established in khul', nor in any contract that does not admit rescission, as in al-Fusul. As for option of defect in the compensation of khul', it is established in cases of serious defect.' His statement: 'and it is confined' from 'iqtisar'; meaning khul' is confined to the session and does not extend beyond it. So if she says: I ransom myself from you for such-and-such, if the husband accepts it in the session in which he learned of it, it is valid; otherwise it is invalid, like sale when the seller offers the sale, the buyer has the right to accept until the session ends; if he accepts after the session changes, it is not valid. His statement: 'acceptance of the husband in the session is necessary'; even if she left the session before his acceptance, it is invalid. Thus in al-'Inayah. And in al-Bada'i': 'The presence of the woman is not required; rather it depends on what is beyond the session, so if she was absent, she may accept when she is informed (of the offer), but in her own session, because it is a transaction on her side.' His statement: 'and an oath' is a conjunction to his statement 'transaction', meaning khul' is an oath from the husband's side because he suspended talaq on the wife's acceptance. His statement: 'so that' is a branching from its being an oath, meaning since it is an oath from his side, the aforementioned rulings that are established from its being a transaction from her side are reversed. His statement: 'meaning if...' etc.: If the husband initiates khul' and says: I ransom you for one thousand dirhams, he does not have the right to retract, nor to rescind it, nor to prevent the woman from accepting. But he may suspend it on a condition or add it to a time, such as: when Zaid comes, then I have ransomed you for such-and-such, or I ransom you for such-and-such tomorrow, or at the beginning of the month, and acceptance is hers after Zaid's arrival or the arrival of the time; because it is a divorce at the occurrence of the condition or time, so her acceptance before that is void. Thus in al-Bada'i'. His statement: 'and stipulation of option for him is not valid': meaning if the husband says: I ransom you for such-and-such on condition that I have the option for three days, then stipulation of option is valid only in transactions, not in relinquishments.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is an expansion on the conditions of khul' (divorce by compensation). It begins by explaining the base text's statement that the wife can retract before acceptance, stipulate an option period for herself, and that acceptance must occur in the same session. The commentator clarifies each point with intra-Hanafi differences.

First, regarding retraction (ruju'): When the woman initiates khul' by offering compensation, she may retract before the husband accepts, analogous to a sale contract where the offeror can withdraw before acceptance.

Second, regarding stipulation of option (khiyar): Abu Hanifa holds that the wife may stipulate a period of option (e.g., three days) to decide whether to proceed with the khul'. If she accepts during that period, talaq occurs and the compensation is due; if she rejects, no talaq occurs. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree: they consider such a stipulation invalid, so talaq occurs immediately with the compensation due. Their reasoning is that option is meant for rescission after conclusion, but khul' from the husband's side is an oath (yamin) that cannot be rescinded, and from the wife's side her acceptance completes the oath. Abu Hanifa's counter-argument is that from the wife's side, khul' is a transaction (mu'awadah) similar to a sale, thus validly admitting option. The commentator notes that only khiyar al-shart (stipulated option) is under discussion; khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) does not apply to khul', but khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) for a serious defect in the compensation is valid.

Third, regarding the session (majlis): The acceptance must occur in the same session as the offer. If the woman initiates and the husband accepts in the same session he learned of it, it is valid; otherwise it is invalid, like a sale offer. However, if the husband initiates, he cannot retract, but the absent wife may accept when she learns of the offer, in her own session (according to al-Bada'i'). This is because the transaction aspect applies to her side.

Finally, the passage emphasizes the dual nature of khul': it is a transaction (mu'awadah) from the wife's side, so she can stipulate conditions and retract before acceptance; it is an oath (yamin) from the husband's side, so he cannot retract once he initiates, and he cannot stipulate an option for himself (since option is only for transactions, not for relinquishments like divorce).

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife with compensation paid to the husband.
خيار (khiyar): An option to confirm or rescind a contract within a specific period.
مجلس (majlis): The session during which the contract offer and acceptance take place.
معاوضة (mu'awadah): A commutative contract involving exchange of consideration.
يمين (yamin): An oath; here referring to the husband's suspension of talaq on the wife's acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the wife initiates khul' and stipulates a three-day option period, which of the following is true?
A) The stipulation is valid per Abu Hanifa, and the wife can reject within the option period.
B) The stipulation is invalid per all Hanafis, and talaq occurs immediately.
C) The husband can also stipulate an option for himself.
D) The khul' is invalid because the offer must be unconditional.

Key Takeaway
In khul', the wife can retract before acceptance, stipulate an option (per Abu Hanifa), and acceptance must be in the same session; the husband cannot retract or stipulate an option for himself because from his side it is an oath.

Acceptance and Option in Khul'
Explains the dual nature and rules of khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules of acceptance and option in khul' based on who initiates, and explain the underlying legal nature (mu'awadah vs yamin) that governs these rules.

Core Idea
Khul' is a transaction for the wife but an oath for the husband — this duality flips the rules of acceptance and option.

Khul' is a unique institution that combines elements of a transaction (mu'awadah) from the wife's side and an oath (yamin) from the husband's side. This dual nature leads to different procedural rules depending on who initiates the khul'. In this lesson, we examine the conditions of acceptance and the prohibition of option, based on the commentary on al-Wiqayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of khul'
Recognise that khul' has two aspects: from the wife's perspective it is a mu'awadah (compensation), from the husband's perspective it is a yamin (suspension of divorce on acceptance). This distinction underpins the divergent rulings.
The text states: 'الخلع يمين في حقه' (khul' is an oath in his regard) and 'معاوضة في جانبها' (compensation from her side).
2
Apply the rule for wife-initiated khul'
When the wife makes the offer, the husband must accept in the same sitting. If the sitting changes, the offer lapses. This mirrors the rules of a sale from the buyer's side (mu'awadah).
The commentator says: 'لا بد من قبول الزوج في المجلس' — the husband's acceptance must be in the sitting.
3
Apply the rule for husband-initiated khul'
When the husband initiates khul', his offer is binding immediately. He cannot revoke it, nor can he stipulate an option (khiyar) for himself. This is because, from his side, it is an oath (yamin) that suspends divorce on the wife's acceptance.
The text says: 'لا يصح رجوعه قبل قبول المرأة، ولا يصح شرط الخيار له' — revocation and option are invalid.
4
Examine the analogy to sale and the exception
The binding nature of the husband's offer is analogous to a sale: when the seller offers, the buyer has the right to accept as long as the sitting continues. However, the husband can condition the khul' on a future event or time, and the wife's acceptance is valid only after that condition/c time arrives.
The passage cites al-Bada'i': the husband may say 'when Zaid arrives' or 'tomorrow' and acceptance after that is valid.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul'. And it is an oath in his [the husband's] regard so that the rulings are reversed. That is, if the offer is from her [the wife], the husband's acceptance is necessary in the sitting. (And it is an oath) in his regard such that the rulings are reversed: That is, if the offer is from his side, his revocation is not valid before the wife's acceptance, and it is not valid to stipulate an option for him. Otherwise it is void, like a sale: if the seller makes an offer, the buyer has the right to accept as long as the sitting continues; if he accepts after the sitting changes, it is not valid. He [the commentator] says: 'It is necessary that the husband accepts in the sitting; even if she leaves the sitting before his acceptance, it is void. Thus in al-'Inayah, and in al-Bada'i': 'The presence of the wife is not a condition, but rather it is contingent on beyond the sitting; even if she is absent, she may accept when the news reaches her, but in her own sitting, because it is a compensation from her side.' He says: 'And it is an oath' is conjuncted to his saying 'compensation', meaning that khul' is an oath with regard to the husband because he suspends divorce on the wife's acceptance. He says: 'such that' is a branching from its being an oath, meaning since it is an oath from his side, the aforementioned rulings that are established from it being a compensation from her side are reversed. He says: 'That is, if... etc.': That is, if the husband initiates khul' and says, 'I divorce you in exchange for a thousand dirhams,' he does not have the power to revoke it, nor does he have the power to annul it, nor can he forbid the wife from accepting. And he may suspend it on a condition or add it to a time, such as 'when Zaid arrives, I divorce you on such and such' or 'I divorce you on such and such tomorrow or at the beginning of the month,' and the acceptance is for her after the arrival of Zaid and the arrival of the time, because it is a divorce at the existence of the condition and time, so her acceptance before that is void. Thus in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the legal nature of khul' (divorce initiated by wife with compensation). The author explains that khul' has dual aspects: from the wife's side it is a mu'awadah (compensation/transaction), and from the husband's side it is a yamin (oath/suspension). This dual nature leads to different rulings depending on who initiates. The author first addresses the scenario where the wife makes the offer: the husband must accept in the same sitting. If he does not, the offer lapses. Then he discusses when the husband initiates: his offer is binding immediately; he cannot revoke it or stipulate an option for himself. This is analogous to a sale where the seller's offer is binding. However, the husband can suspend the khul' on a condition or defer it to a future time, and the wife's acceptance is valid only after the condition is fulfilled or the time arrives. The commentary cites al-Bada'i' for this ruling. The key point is that the rulings of khul' are 'reversed' depending on whose side is considered: the wife's side is like a sale, while the husband's side is like an oath. This understanding is essential for correctly applying the rules of khul'.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): A form of divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation, which combines aspects of a transaction and an oath.
يمين (yamin): An oath; in this context, the husband's suspension of divorce on the wife's acceptance is considered a legal oath.
معاوضة (mu'awadah): A commutative contract where a benefit is exchanged for compensation; the wife's payment in khul' is considered a mu'awadah from her side.
خيار (khiyar): The option to rescind or confirm a contract; the husband cannot stipulate an option in khul'.
مجلس (majlis): The sitting where the contract is concluded; acceptance must occur in the same sitting as the offer when the wife initiates khul'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The husband can stipulate an option for himself.
B) The husband cannot stipulate an option, and his offer is binding.
C) The wife can stipulate an option.
D) Both parties can stipulate an option.

Key Takeaway
Khul' is a transaction from the wife's side and an oath from the husband's side; this duality determines whether acceptance must be in the same sitting (wife initiates) or if the husband's offer is binding without option (husband initiates).

The Dual Nature of Khul': Compensation and Oath
Explains the dual aspects of khul' and compares it to slave manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dual legal nature of khul' (compensation from the wife, oath from the husband) and apply the analogy of qisas to defend the validity of compensating for non-property rights.

Core Idea
Khul' is simultaneously a compensation from the wife and an oath from the husband, justified by analogy to qisas.

This passage delves into the composite nature of khul'. While it resembles a sale from the wife's perspective, it also operates as an oath from the husband's side. Understanding this duality is key to appreciating why khul' is valid even though marriage ownership is not property. The author defends this using a powerful analogy from qisas (retaliation), another non-property right that can be exchanged for wealth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Aspects of Khul'
From the wife's side, she gives wealth to secure release from marriage — this is a compensation. From the husband's side, he suspends talaq on her acceptance — this is an oath (yamin). State clearly what each aspect entails.
The oath aspect explains why acceptance may occur after the sitting: an oath is not immediately binding like a contract.
2
Address the Key Objection
A critic may argue: 'Marriage ownership (milk al-nikah) is not property, so how can it be exchanged for money?' Pinpoint exactly why this objection is raised and why it challenges khul' validity.
This objection stems from a narrow view of what can be subject to a commutative contract.
3
Follow the Qisas Analogy
The author responds by citing qisas: the right to retaliation is not property, yet Quran (2:178) permits commuting it for blood money (diyah). Identify the common illa: both are non-property rights that the Lawgiver allows to be exchanged for wealth.
This is a qiyas shabahah (analogy of resemblance) based on a shared attribute.
4
Compare with Slave Manumission
The passage ends by likening the wife's side to a slave's position in manumission for payment. In both cases, the person gives money to secure freedom. Note how this reinforces the compensation aspect from the wife's perspective.
This comparison helps illustrate that compensation for personal freedom is legally recognized.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If you say: 'The ownership of marriage is not wealth, so how can compensation be valid in exchange for it?' I say: 'Compensation for that which is not wealth is also valid, as in qisas, for it is not wealth but a right established for the heirs of the slain person against the killer, yet compensation for it with wealth is valid by the text of the Quran.' As for the second, because the husband suspends divorce on the woman's acceptance, so it becomes a suspension from his side, which is what is called an oath. He says: 'And the slave's side in manumission...' meaning in manumission for wealth, since the slave gives wealth and surrenders it to the master to secure his person and to be released from the master's ownership, and the master suspends manumission on the slave's acceptance, so the wealth becomes binding on him, and there is no binding without acceptance and commitment, so that becomes a compensation from the slave's side, just as khul' is a compensation from the wife's side.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the legal nature of khul'. The author clarifies that khul' is not restricted to the sitting when the husband makes the offer; the wife can accept after leaving the sitting. This is because from the husband's side it is an oath, not a binding transaction. The author then explains why khul' has both aspects: from the wife's side, she gives wealth to secure her freedom from marriage, so it is a compensation (mu'awadah). From the husband's side, he suspends divorce on her acceptance, so it is an oath (yamin). The author addresses a potential objection: marriage is not property, so how can compensation be valid? He responds by citing the example of qisas (retaliation), which is not property but can be commuted for blood money by Qur'anic authority. This shows that compensation for non-property rights is valid. Finally, the author draws an analogy between khul' and a slave's manumission for payment. Just as the slave gives money to be freed, from his side it is a compensation; similarly, the wife gives money to be divorced. This comparison reinforces the dual nature of khul' and helps understand the rulings.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation for murder or injury; a right of the victim's heirs that is not property but can be commuted for blood money.
عتاق (itaq): Manumission of a slave; in this context, manumission in exchange for payment by the slave.
ملك النكاح (milk al-nikah): The husband's ownership of the marriage bond, which is a legal right but not considered property (mal) in the sense of fungible wealth.

Disputed Acceptance in Khul' vs. Sale
Comparing rulings on disputed acceptance in khul' and sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on disputed acceptance in khul' from that in sale, and explain the legal basis for the difference using the concepts of yamin and mu'awada.

Core Idea
In khul', the husband's word prevails; in sale, the buyer's word prevails—because khul' is an oath, sale is an exchange.

This passage explores a nuanced legal distinction: when a husband claims he divorced his wife for a sum but she did not accept, and she claims she accepted, whose word is taken? The Hanafi school answers: the husband's. But in a similar sale dispute, the buyer's word is taken. Why the difference? The key lies in the legal nature of khul' as an oath (yamin) from the husband's side, and sale as a pure exchange (mu'awada). Let's trace the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dispute scenarios
First, note the two scenarios: (1) Husband says he divorced his wife for a sum, but claims she did not accept; wife claims she accepted. (2) Seller says he sold an item but the buyer did not accept; buyer claims he accepted.
Both involve a claim that acceptance did or did not occur.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
Ruling: In khul', the husband's word is taken. In sale, the buyer's word is taken. This is the core procedural outcome.
3
Understand the nature of khul'
Khul' is primarily an oath (yamin) from the husband's side. The divorce itself (the oath) is not conditional on the wife's acceptance, although the financial consideration (badal) is. Therefore, the husband's statement 'I divorced you for a sum' does not imply that the wife accepted.
In contrast, absolute divorce does not require any acceptance.
4
Understand the nature of sale
Sale is an exchange (mu'awada) that requires both offer and acceptance as pillars. The seller's statement 'I sold' inherently implies that the buyer accepted, because sale cannot be completed without acceptance.
5
Apply the legal distinction
Because the husband's statement does not imply acceptance, when he denies acceptance he is not contradicting his own earlier statement; he is simply denying a separate condition. Hence his word is taken as the denier. In sale, the seller's statement implies acceptance, so denying acceptance would be a retraction of his own admission, which is not allowed. Hence the buyer's word is taken.
This difference arises from the legal characterization of each transaction.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul'. If he said: 'I divorced you yesterday for one thousand and you did not accept,' and she said: 'I accepted,' then the statement is his. So it is from the slave's side a compensation (mu'awada) and from the master's side an oath (yamin), and it is a conditional emancipation upon the slave's acceptance, so the rulings of compensation apply on the slave's side, not on the master's side. And if he said: 'I divorced you yesterday for one thousand and you did not accept,' and she said: 'I accepted,' then the statement is hers, the woman, and an oath from the master's side, just as khul' is an oath on his side, so the rulings related to the oath and to compensation apply in each appropriate aspect. [1] His saying: 'upon condition of the slave's acceptance' – for emancipation for money is not realized without his acceptance, although absolute emancipation is realized without it, just as khul' is not realized without the woman's acceptance, although absolute divorce is realized without it. [2] His saying: 'rulings of compensation' – such as validity of revocation, validity of stipulating an option, and restriction to the session. [3] His saying: 'not on the master's side' – because his side is the side of oath, so the rulings of compensation do not apply from his side, but rather the rulings of the oath. [4] His saying: 'yesterday' – with fatha on the hamza and kasra on the seen, with a sukoon on the meem between them – it is a name for the past day. The origin of the issue: if the husband mentions the occurrence of divorce from him in the past with the absence of acceptance from both, and the woman claims her acceptance, then the statement is his. Similarly the ruling if the master said to his slave: 'I emancipated you yesterday for one thousand and you did not accept,' or said: 'I sold you yourself from me yesterday for one thousand and you did not accept.' Thus in 'al-Bahr'. (1) [0] His saying: 'the statement is his' – meaning the statement is the husband's word along with his oath, for he is the denier and the woman is the claimant. If she proves her claim with evidence, then that. If she does not provide proof and asks him to swear, he swears. If he swears, her claim is nullified. If he refuses, it is realized. If both present evidence, her evidence is taken that she accepted, because the principle is that the one whose word is accepted does not need evidence, for evidence is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the word is for the one whom the apparent supports. (1) 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (4:93). And if the seller said likewise, then the statement is for the buyer, and the khul' drops. And if the seller said likewise, then the statement is for the buyer – meaning if the seller said: 'I sold this slave to you yesterday for one thousand dirhams and you did not accept,' and the buyer said: 'I accepted,' then the statement is for the buyer. And the explanation of the difference: the seller's statement 'I sold' is an acknowledgement of the buyer's acceptance, because a sale is not valid except with offer and acceptance, so his statement 'you did not accept' is a retraction of his acknowledgement, unlike khul', for it is an oath on his side, so it can be separated from the consideration, hence it is not an acknowledgement of the woman's acceptance, so his statement is his word because he is the denier of khul' and the woman claims it. [0] (And the khul' drops). And here the husband is denier of the existence of the condition of breach (hanth), which is acceptance, and the woman's statement contradicts the apparent, so her evidence is preferred when conflicting. If you say: How is the husband's evidence accepted when it is based on negation, and evidence is only accepted when it is based on affirmation, not negation? I say: Evidence on negation in the condition of breach is acceptable, as established in its place. (1) His saying: 'and if the seller said' – meaning if such a dispute occurs in the chapter of sale between seller and buyer, the statement is the buyer's. [2] His saying: 'the statement is for the buyer' – if the seller presents evidence for his claim, then that, and he does not swear. [3] His saying: 'indeed the seller's statement ... etc.' – its summary: it is established that a sale is not realized except with offer and acceptance, for they are its two pillars, and it is not realized by mere offer. So the seller's statement 'I sold' implies an acknowledgement of the buyer's acceptance, so after that his statement 'you did not accept' is a retraction of his acknowledgement, thus it is not heard. As for khul', it is an oath on his side, and it is a complete contract that does not depend on the woman's acceptance, so his statement 'I divorced you yesterday for one thousand' is not an acknowledgement of her acceptance, so his statement 'you did not accept' is not a retraction of it, therefore it is not heard. [4] His saying: 'drops' – present tense verb from isqat, and what follows is its subject. [0] His saying: 'khul'' – not divorce for money according to the relied-upon view, as in 'al-Bazzaziyya'.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof comparing two similar but legally distinct scenarios: disputed acceptance in khul' (divorce for consideration) and in sale. The author begins by stating the ruling: if a husband says he divorced his wife yesterday for a sum but she did not accept, and the wife claims she accepted, the husband's word is taken. He then explains the nature of khul' from the perspective of a slave's emancipation for money analogy — it is both a compensation (mu'awada) from the slave's side and an oath (yamin) from the master's side. The text clarifies that khul' is like emancipation for money: it does not become effective without the wife's acceptance, though absolute divorce would be effective without it. Rulings of compensation (e.g., right of revocation, stipulating an option, being confined to the session) apply only on the side where it is a compensation, not on the side where it is an oath. The passage then explains the meaning of 'yesterday' and extends the same ruling to the case of a master emancipating his slave for money or selling the slave his own person — in those cases too, the master's word is taken. The author then addresses the procedure: the husband (or master) is the denier, the woman (or slave) is the claimant. If the claimant proves her acceptance with evidence, it is accepted; otherwise the defendant must swear an oath; if he swears, the claim fails; if he refuses, the claim is established. If both present evidence, the woman's evidence is preferred because the husband's word is the apparent. The passage then contrasts this with a sale scenario: if a seller says he sold an item yesterday and the buyer did not accept, but the buyer claims he accepted, the buyer's word is taken. The reason is that the seller's statement 'I sold' is an acknowledgement that the buyer accepted, because sale requires both offer and acceptance; therefore retracting that acknowledgement is not allowed. In khul', however, the husband's statement 'I divorced you for a sum' is not an acknowledgement of the wife's acceptance; it is an oath that can be separated from the consideration. Hence the husband can deny acceptance, and his word is taken as the denier. The passage concludes by discussing the acceptance of evidence of negation in the condition of breach (hanth) — it is acceptable, contrary to the general rule that evidence is for affirmation. Finally, the author notes that 'khul'' in this context means the relied-upon view that it is khul' proper, not divorce for money (which has different rulings).

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for a consideration (usually financial) given to the husband. In this passage, it is characterized as an oath (yamin) on the husband's side.
المعاوضة (al-mu'awada): Compensation or exchange; in this context, it refers to the financial aspect of the transaction where the wife gives something to the husband in exchange for divorce.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; here it describes the legal nature of khul' from the husband's perspective — it is a binding oath that effects divorce, not a contract dependent on acceptance.
القبول (al-qabul): Acceptance; in khul', the wife's acceptance is necessary for the consideration but not for the divorce itself. In sale, the buyer's acceptance is essential for the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale dispute where the seller says 'I sold the item yesterday and you did not accept' and the buyer says 'I accepted,' whose word is taken according to the Hanafi school?
A) The seller's word, because he is the offeror.
B) The buyer's word, because the seller's statement implies acceptance.
C) The word of both, and the case is dismissed.
D) The word of a judge, because it is a disputed matter.

Key Takeaway
The distinction hinges on whether acceptance is integral to the contract: in khul', divorce is independent of acceptance, so the husband's statement does not admit acceptance; in sale, acceptance is integral, so the seller's statement admits it.

Whose Word Is Taken: Sale vs. Khul'
Ruling on whose statement is accepted in a dispute over acceptance in sale vs. khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school credits the buyer's word in a sale dispute and the husband's word in a khul' dispute, and identify the underlying rationale based on the legal nature of each transaction.

Core Idea
The seller's claim of non-acceptance contradicts his own acknowledgment; in khul', the husband's statement is an oath independent of acceptance.

In Islamic jurisprudence, when parties disagree about whether acceptance occurred in a contract, legal presumptions determine whose statement is credited. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya compares two key scenarios: a sale and a khul' divorce. While both involve an offer and a claimed acceptance, the Hanafi school treats them differently because of their distinct legal natures. Understanding this distinction deepens your grasp of how the madhhab reasons from contract structure to procedural rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dispute scenarios
First, note the two cases: (1) A seller claims he sold an item but the buyer did not accept; the buyer says he accepted. (2) A husband claims he performed khul' but the wife did not accept; the wife says she accepted.
The same factual pattern appears, but the rulings differ.
2
Analyze the legal nature of sale
A sale contract requires both offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) as pillars. Therefore, when the seller says 'I sold', his statement inherently acknowledges that acceptance occurred – otherwise the sale would be invalid. His later denial of acceptance is a retraction of that acknowledgment.
This is why the seller cannot simply deny acceptance; he is contradicting his own prior statement.
3
Analyze the legal nature of khul'
In Hanafi law, khul' is categorized as a yamin (oath) from the husband's side. An oath is complete upon utterance and does not require the wife's acceptance to be binding. Hence, the husband's statement 'I divorced you for 1000' does not imply that the wife accepted.
The khul' can be valid even without her acceptance if it is considered an oath.
4
Apply the rule of qawl (whose statement is credited)
In disputes, the default is that the party who denies the claim (the one who says the event did not happen) has the burden of proof. In sale, the buyer's claim of acceptance is the default because the seller's statement implied it; thus the buyer's word is taken. In khul', the husband denies the acceptance, so his word is taken.
The burden of proof is on the party whose claim contradicts the apparent legal implication.
5
Consider the evidence issue
The passage addresses a potential objection: can the husband bring evidence of negation (that she did not accept)? The answer is yes, when the negation concerns a condition of an oath, such evidence is admissible. This is a technical exception.
This shows the depth of procedural rules in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال البائع كذلك فالقولُ للمشتري ، ويسقط الخلع ولو قال البائع كذلك فالقولُ للمشتري) : أي إذا قال البائع : بعث هذا العبد منك بألف درهم أمس، فلم تقبل، وقال المشتري : قبلت ، فالقول للمشتري. ووجه الفرق : أن قول البائع : بعتُ ؛ إقرار بقبول المشتري ؛ لأن البيع لا يصح إلا بالإيجاب والقبول، فقوله : فلم تقبل يكون رجوعاً عن إقراره بخلاف الخلع، فإنه يمين في حقه، فيمكن إنفكاكه عن البدل، فلا يكون إقراراً بقَبُول المرأة، فيكون القول قولُه ؛ لأنَّه منكر للخلع، والمرأة تدعيه. And if the seller says likewise, then the word is for the buyer, and the khul' falls. And if the seller says likewise, then the word is for the buyer: Meaning, if the seller says: 'I sold this slave to you for one thousand dirhams yesterday, and you did not accept,' and the buyer says: 'I accepted,' then the word is for the buyer. Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the ruling when there is a dispute between parties regarding acceptance in a sale contract versus a khul' divorce. The matn states: 'If the seller says likewise, then the word is for the buyer, and the khul' falls.' The commentator clarifies that if a seller claims he sold an item for a price but the buyer did not accept, while the buyer says he did accept, the buyer's word is taken. The reason is that the seller's statement 'I sold' implies an acknowledgment that the buyer accepted, because a sale requires both offer and acceptance. Therefore, the seller cannot later retract that acknowledgment by denying acceptance. In contrast, in khul', the husband's statement 'I divorce you for a compensation' is considered an oath (yamin) on his part. This oath can be separated from the compensation, meaning the divorce could occur without the compensation if the wife does not accept. Hence, the husband's statement does not imply an acknowledgment that the wife accepted. So if the husband claims he divorced her but she did not accept, his word is taken because he denies the khul' while the woman claims it. The passage then addresses a potential objection: how can the husband's evidence (that she did not accept) be accepted when it is negative evidence? The answer is that evidence of negation regarding a condition of an oath (here, the acceptance as a condition for the husband's oath to be triggered) is acceptable, as established elsewhere.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
القول (al-qawl): The party whose statement is credited in a dispute; the burden of proof is on the one who claims contrary to the default.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment of a fact; in this context, the seller's statement 'I sold' is an acknowledgment of the buyer's acceptance.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; here, the husband's statement of khul' is considered an oath, making it independent of the wife's acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between a seller and buyer over whether the buyer accepted the offer, whose word is accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) The seller's word is accepted.
B) The buyer's word is accepted.
C) Both parties' words are equally considered.
D) The dispute is settled by oath from both.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between sale and khul' based on legal nature: sale requires acceptance, so the seller's statement implies it; khul' is an oath, so it does not.

Mubara'ah and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Forfeited Rights
Definition of mubara'ah and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on forfeited rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of mubara'ah, explain the conditions for isqat, and evaluate the three Hanafi positions on forfeited rights in khul' and mubara'ah.

Core Idea
The wording and compensation in mubara'ah determine whether all rights are forfeited or only those stipulated.

In Hanafi law, mubara'ah is a form of mutual divorce where both spouses release each other from all rights. However, the precise effect—whether all rights or only specified ones are forfeited—was disputed among the founding imams. This lesson unpacks the definition, the role of wording (mufa'alah vs. others), and the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and key terms
Read the passage to understand that mubara'ah is defined on the pattern of mufa'alah (mutual action). Note that it is compared to khul' and that the wording 'bara'tuka' is used. Recognize the condition that if the wording is not mufa'alah and no compensation is mentioned, it is not isqat but a ba'in talaq.
The text cites al-Mughrib and al-Binayah for linguistic points.
2
Distinguish the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad
The passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Abu Hanifa: both khul' and mubara'ah forfeit all rights. Abu Yusuf: mubara'ah forfeits all rights, but khul' only stipulated rights. Muhammad: neither forfeits rights beyond what is explicitly mentioned.
Use the key terms 'isqat' (forfeiture) and 'mu'awadah' (commutative contract).
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil) behind each position
Abu Hanifa argues that khul' implies complete separation (fisal) from the marriage bond and its rights. Abu Yusuf notes that mubara'ah derives from bara'ah (release), so it inherently releases both sides, whereas khul' only implies dissolution. Muhammad views both as commutative contracts where only stipulated terms matter.
Supporting citations: al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah, al-Fatawa al-Sughra, Jami' al-Fusulayn, al-Khaniyyah.
4
Understand the secondary dispute on sale/purchase wording
The passage also records a difference regarding khul' expressed with sale language (e.g., 'I sold you yourself'). Some later authorities (e.g., Sahib al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah) treat it as isqat, while others (e.g., Sahib Jami' al-Fusulayn) treat it as non-forfeiture unless specified.
This shows that the dispute extends beyond the foundational imams to later fatwa works.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
The reliance on Abu Hanifa's view is evident from the text's statement that in al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah and al-Fatawa al-Sughra, the sale-wording is considered isqat, aligning with Abu Hanifa. Thus the operative position is that all rights are forfeited in khul' and mubara'ah unless otherwise stipulated.
However, students should note that the ikhtilaf is legitimate and may be considered in tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الخلع والمباراة كل حق لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما على الآخر. والمباراة كل حق لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما على الآخر. قوله : والمبارأة ; على وزن المفاعلة ، يقال : برأ شريكه ; أي أبرأ كلّ واحدٍ منهما صاحبه وهي بالهمزة، قال في المغرب: ترك الهمز فيه خطأ. كذا في البناية. والحاصل : إن لفظ المبارأة يقوم مقام الخلع ، كأن يقول : بارأتك وتقبله هي ، أو يقول : برأت من نكاحك بألف ، ولو قال : برئت من نكاحك بلا ذكر المبدل يقع به الطلاق إذا نوى، ولا يكون خُلعاً. وبالجملة : إذا لم يكن بلفظ المفاعلة ولم يذكر بدلاً لم يتوقف على قبولها فيقع به البائن، ولا يكون مسقطاً بمنزلة قوله : خلعتك، بخلاف ما إذا كان بلفظ المفاعلة أو ذكر له بدلاً ; فإنه يتوقف على القبول حتى يكون مسقطاً. كذا في رد المحتار. واختلفوا في الخلع بلفظ البيع والشراء، كأن يقول : بعت منك نفسك بكذا ، وتقول هي : اشتريت، فصحح صاحب الفصول العمادية، والفتاوى الصغرى: إنّه مسقط، كالخلع والمبارأة، وصحح صاحب جامع الفصولين والخانية: إنه غير مسقط للمهر وغيره إلا بذكره. قوله : كل حق ... الخ ; هذا عنده، وقال محمد : لا يسقط فيهما إلا ما سمياه، وأبو يوسف يقولُ بسقوط كل حق في المباراة. وجه قول محمد له : إن الخلع والمباراة معاوضة، وفي المعاوضات يعتبر المشروط لا غيره. ووجه قول أبي يوسف : أن المبارأة مفاعلة من البراءة، فتقتضيها من الجانبين، وأمّا الخلع فمقتضاه الانخلاع ، وقد حصل بنقض النكاح. ولأبي حنيفة : إن الخلع ينبئ عن الفصل، ولا فصل إلا عن وصل، ولا وصل إلا بالنكاح ، وحقوقه اللازمة، وقد وقع مطلقاً من غير قيد بالنكاح، فيعملُ بالإطلاق كما في المبارأة في النكاح وأحكامه وحقوقه قولاً بكمال الفصل، وإنما لا
The Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul' and Mubara'ah: Every right of each one of them against the other. And mubara'ah: every right of each one of them against the other. He said: 'Al-Mubara'ah' on the pattern of al-mufa'alah, one says: bar'a shareekahu, meaning each of them declared his companion free, and it is with hamzah. He said in al-Mughrib: 'Dropping the hamzah is an error.' Likewise in al-Binayah. The summary: The word mubara'ah serves the same function as khul', like if he says: 'I have declared you free (bara'tuka)' and she accepts, or he says: 'I have declared myself free from your marriage for one thousand,' and if he says: 'I have declared myself free from your marriage' without mentioning the compensation, talaq falls if he intends it, and it is not a khul'. In general, if it is not in the wording of mufa'alah and he does not mention a compensation, it does not depend on her acceptance, and a ba'in talaq occurs, and it is not a forfeiture of rights like when he says 'khala'tuka', unlike if it is with the wording of mufa'alah or he mentions a compensation; then it depends on acceptance until it is a forfeiture. ... They differed regarding khul' with the wording of sale and purchase, like if he says: 'I have sold you yourself for such-and-such' and she says 'I have bought', then the author of al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah and al-Fatawa al-Sughra corrected that it is a forfeiture, like khul' and mubara'ah, and the author of Jami' al-Fusulayn and al-Khaniyyah corrected that it is not a forfeiture of the dower or other than it unless it is mentioned. He said: 'Every right...' etc.: This is his (Abu Hanifa's) position. Muhammad said: 'Nothing is forfeited in either except what they specify.' Abu Yusuf said: 'Every right is forfeited in mubara'ah.' The reasoning for Muhammad's statement: Khul' and mubara'ah are commutative contracts, and in commutative contracts only that which is stipulated is considered, nothing else. The reasoning for Abu Yusuf's statement: Mubara'ah is a mufa'alah from bara'ah (release), so it demands release from both sides. As for khul', its demand is separation, and that has been achieved by the dissolution of the marriage. For Abu Hanifa: Khul' indicates separation, and there is no separation except from a bond, and there is no bond except marriage and its necessary rights, and it has occurred absolutely without being restricted to the marriage, so it is given effect absolutely, just as in mubara'ah, in the marriage, its rulings, and its rights, as a statement of complete separation, and it is not...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that defines mubara'ah as a mutual release of all rights. The commentary explains that mubara'ah is similar to khul' but with specific wording. It then presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds that in both khul' and mubara'ah, all rights are forfeited because the word khul' implies complete separation from the marriage bond and its necessary rights. Abu Yusuf differentiates: in mubara'ah, all rights are forfeited because the word itself implies mutual release, but in khul' only stipulated rights are forfeited. Muhammad holds that in both only the rights explicitly mentioned are forfeited because these are commutative contracts. The passage also notes a secondary dispute regarding khul' using sale/purchase terminology. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the wording matters: if it is in the form of mufa'alah or mentions compensation, it requires acceptance and is a forfeiture (isqat); otherwise, it is a ba'in talaq without forfeiture of rights.

Key Terms
المبارأة (al-mubara'ah): A form of divorce by mutual consent where both spouses release each other from all rights, using the wording of mufa'alah (mutual release).
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation; here discussed regarding the extent of forfeited rights.
الإسقاط (al-isqat): Forfeiture of rights; a khul' or mubara'ah that is isqat means all marital rights (like dower) are dropped.
المعاوضة (al-mu'awadah): A commutative contract where each party gives something in exchange; Muhammad views khul' and mubara'ah as such.
المفاعلة (al-mufa'alah): A verbal form indicating an action done mutually; here, the wording 'بارأتك' is an example.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the effect of a khul' that does not specify which rights are forfeited?
A) Only the dower is forfeited.
B) All rights of the wife against the husband are forfeited.
C) All rights of both parties are forfeited.
D) Only rights mentioned in the khul' are forfeited.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the extent of forfeited rights in khul' and mubara'ah depends on the wording and whether the imam views it as isqat or mu'awadah; the mufta bihi is that all rights are forfeited.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Definition of Mubara'a and Its Equivalence to Khul'
Defines mubara'a and its legal equivalence to khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mubara'a, distinguish its conditions for being equivalent to khul', and apply the ruling to different wordings.

Core Idea
Mubara'a is mutual release; its ruling hinges on wording (mufa'alah) and compensation.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi definition of mubara'a—a form of marital dissolution where both spouses release their mutual rights. The passage explains how the wording and presence of a substitute determine whether mubara'a functions like khul' (a revocable talaq) or a repudiation (isqat). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly categorizing divorce pronouncements in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
Mubara'a is a mutual release of rights between spouses. Note its linguistic form (mufa'alah) and that it is equivalent to khul' in legal effect.
The term 'mubara'a' is on the pattern of mufa'alah, implying reciprocity.
2
Examine the Conditions for Khul' Equivalence
Mubara'a is equivalent to khul' if either: (a) the husband uses the mufa'alah wording (e.g., 'bāra'tuk') OR (b) he mentions a substitute (compensation). In both cases, the wife's acceptance is required, and it is a repudiation (isqat).
Example: 'I have made a mutual release with you for one thousand.'
3
Analyze Non-Mufa'alah Wording without Compensation
If the husband says 'bari'tu min nikāḥik' (I am free from your marriage) without compensation and without intending khul', it is considered a revocable talaq if he intended divorce, not a khul'.
This statement does not require the wife's acceptance and is not a repudiation.
4
Summarize the Ruling
The key distinction: if the wording is not mufa'alah and no substitute is mentioned, it is a revocable talaq (bā'in) dependent on intent. Otherwise, it is a repudiation requiring acceptance.
The mufta bihi is clear: mufa'alah or substitute → acceptance needed; otherwise → talaq if intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب الخلع والمباراة كل حق لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما على الآخر. قوله : والمبارأة ؛ على وزن المفاعلة ، يقال : برأ شريكه ؛ أي أبرأ كلّ واحدٍ منهما صاحبه وهي بالهمزة، قال في «المغرب»: «ترك الهمز فيه خطأ. كذا في «البناية». والحاصل : إن لفظ المبارأة يقوم مقام الخلع ، كأن يقول : بارأتك وتقبله هي ، أو يقول : برأت من نكاحك بألف ، ولو قال : برئت من نكاحك بلا ذكر المبدل يقع به الطلاق إذا نوى، ولا يكون خُلعاً. وبالجملة : إذا لم يكن بلفظ المفاعلة ولم يذكر بدلاً لم يتوقف على قبولها فيقع به البائن، ولا يكون مسقطاً بمنزلة قوله : خلعتك، بخلاف ما إذا كان بلفظ المفاعلة أو ذكر له بدلاً ؛ فإنه يتوقف على القبول حتى يكون مسقطاً. كذا في رد المحتار). (۱) انتهى من «المغرب» (ص ٤٠). (۲) «البناية (٤) : ٦٨١). (۳) رد المحتار» (٣: ٤٥٣).
The book of divorce, chapter of khul'. And mubara'a is every right that each of them has against the other. His statement: 'And al-mubara'ah' is on the pattern of mufa'alah; it is said: 'bara'a sharikahu' meaning each one of them acquitted his companion, and it is with hamzah. He said in al-Maghrib: 'Omitting the hamzah in it is a mistake.' Likewise in al-Binayah. The summary: The word mubara'ah takes the place of khul'. For example, he says: 'I have made a mutual release with you' and she accepts it, or he says: 'I have acquitted myself from your marriage for one thousand.' And if he said: 'I am free from your marriage' without mentioning the compensation, talaq occurs if he intended, and it is not khul'. In short, if it is not with the wording of mufa'alah and he did not mention a substitute, it is not dependent on her acceptance, rather it is revocable divorce, and it is not a repudiation like his saying: 'I have divorced you.' Contrary to when it is with the wording of mufa'alah or he mentioned a substitute, then it is dependent on acceptance so that it is a repudiation. Likewise in Radd al-Muhtar. (1) Ended from al-Maghrib (p. 40). (2) al-Binayah (4:681). (3) Radd al-Muhtar (3:453).
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement. It begins by defining mubara'a as a mutual release of rights, explaining its linguistic form. The author clarifies that mubara'a is equivalent to khul' in legal effect, but the wording and presence of compensation determine whether it is a repudiation (isqat) or a revocable talaq. If the husband uses the mufa'alah form (like 'bara'tuk') or mentions a substitute, the woman's acceptance is necessary and it functions as a release. If he simply says 'bari'tu min nikahik' without compensation, it is considered a revocable talaq if he intends divorce, not a khul'.

Key Terms
مبارأة (mubāraʾah): A mutual release of rights between spouses, functioning like khul' but with different conditions on wording.
خلع (khulʿ): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation (usually the mahr).
مفاعلة (mufāʿalah): The verbal form indicating reciprocity (like bāraʾa yubāriʾu).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does mubara'a require the wife's acceptance to be valid?
A) Only when the husband uses the word 'khul''
B) When the husband uses the mufa'alah form OR mentions a substitute
C) Always, regardless of wording
D) Never, it is always effective without acceptance

Key Takeaway
Mubara'a's ruling hinges on wording: mufa'alah or compensation requires acceptance (khul'), otherwise it is revocable talaq if intended.
